Elementary Differential Geometry
The link between the physical world and its visualisation is geometry. This easy-to-read,
generously illustrated textbook presents an elementary introduction to differential
geometry with emphasis on geometric results. Avoiding formalism as much as possi-
ble, the author harnesses basic mathematical skills in analysis and linear algebra to
solve interesting geometric problems, which prepare students for more advanced study
in mathematics and other scientiÔ¨Åc Ô¨Åelds such as physics and computer science.
The wide range of topics includes curve theory, a detailed study of surfaces, cur-
vature, variation of area and minimal surfaces, geodesics, spherical and hyperbolic
geometry, the divergence theorem, triangulations, and the Gauss‚ÄìBonnet theorem.
The section on cartography demonstrates the concrete importance of elementary dif-
ferential geometry in applications. Clearly developed arguments and proofs, colour
illustrations, and over 100 exercises and solutions make this book ideal for courses and
self-study. The only prerequisites are one year of undergraduate calculus and linear
algebra.
Christian B√§r is Professor of Geometry in the Institute for Mathematics at the
University of Potsdam, Germany.

Preface
This book evolved from courses about elementary differential geometry which
I have taught in Freiburg, Hamburg and Potsdam. The word ‚Äúelemen-
tary‚Äù should not be understood as ‚Äúparticularly easy‚Äù, but indicates that the
development of formalism, which would be necessary for a deeper study of dif-
ferential geometry, is avoided as much as possible. We will instead approach
geometrically interesting problems using tools from the standard fundamen-
tal courses in analysis and linear algebra. It is possible to raise interesting
questions even about objects as ‚Äúsimple‚Äù as plane curves. The proof of the
four-vertex theorem, for example, is anything but trivial.
The book is suitable for students from the second year of study onwards
and can be used in lectures, seminars, or for private study.
The Ô¨Årst chapter is interesting mostly for historical reasons. The reader can
here Ô¨Ånd out how geometric results have been obtained from axioms for thou-
sands of years, since Euclid. In particular, the controversy about the parallel
axiom will be explained. In this chapter we will mostly follow Hilbert‚Äôs pre-
sentation of plane geometry, since it is rather close to Euclid‚Äôs formulation of
the axioms and yet meets today‚Äôs requirements for mathematical rigour. In
the mean time the axiomatic system has been simpliÔ¨Åed signiÔ¨Åcantly [2]. A
presentation with only seven axioms can be found in [30].
Anyone who is only interested in differential geometry can begin with the
second chapter. The theory of curves is developed here, with particular focus
on curves in the plane and in three-dimensional space. Curvature, a central
notion in differential geometry, appears for the Ô¨Årst time. Of particular interest
are global results, i.e. statements about the overall shape of closed curves. The
above-mentioned four-vertex theorem and the theorems of Fenchel and F√°ry‚Äì
Milnor fall into this category. They tell us how much a space curve needs to
curve so that it can close up (Fenchel) and how much a curve needs to curve
to become knotted (F√°ry‚ÄìMilnor).
We begin to study surfaces in three-dimensional space in the third chapter.
The necessary concepts are introduced, e.g. different notions of curvature, and
vii

viii
PREFACE
some important classes of surfaces are studied in more detail. One of them
is the class of minimal surfaces, which appear in nature as soap Ô¨Ålms. Some
examples are illustrated in colour as well.
In the fourth chapter we change our point of view and concentrate on
geometric quantities that can be obtained using measurements taken on the
surface itself only. We study the shortest connecting curves between two
points on a given surface, for example. This stance suggests the introduction
of general Riemannian metrics, which allows us to construct new important
geometries. The most prominent example is the hyperbolic plane, which, as
Hilbert showed, cannot be realised as a ‚Äúclassical‚Äù surface. One reason why
the hyperbolic plane is so important is that it ended the controversy about
the parallel axiom. It is therefore often referred to as a non-Euclidean geom-
etry. We devote ourselves to hyperbolic and spherical geometry and derive
the most important trigonometric laws. Spherical geometry is used to discuss
applications in cartography. We conclude the chapter with a comparison of
different models of hyperbolic geometry illustrated by a woodcut of Dutch
artist M. C. Escher.
In the Ô¨Åfth chapter we derive Gauss‚Äôs divergence theorem and deduce that
the total Gauss curvature of a closed surface does not depend on the Rie-
mannian metric. The total curvature is thus a ‚Äútopological invariant‚Äù of the
surface.
The last chapter is dedicated to the topological interpretation of this quan-
tity. We show that every compact surface can be triangulated, i.e. that it can be
cut into triangles in a suitable way. The Gauss‚ÄìBonnet theorem then tells us
that the total curvature can be found by counting vertices, edges and triangles.
We conclude with the outlook and recommendations for further study.
Three appendices follow: Ô¨Årst hints for solutions to the exercises, then a col-
lection of useful formulae concerned with the inner geometry of surfaces and
the most important trigonometric laws, and Ô¨Ånally, the mathematical symbols
used in this book are listed, to make it easier to look them up. As is the custom,
the book ends with the references and the index.
The enumeration of theorems, lemmas, examples and so forth is done using
three numbers, where the Ô¨Årst one denotes the chapter and the second one
the section. The numerous exercises are enumerated by two numbers, the Ô¨Årst
one being the chapter. They are interspersed in the text and mainly discuss
examples, which can be used to practice the material treated so far. The fur-
ther logical arguments do not build on most exercises, but reading and doing
the exercises is recommended to establish the necessary familiarity with the
introduced concepts.
At this point it is my pleasure to thank all those that made this book
a success, e.g. by pointing out mistakes and making suggestions, including
B. Ammann, M. Aubel, F. Auer, L. Au√üenhofer, P. Ghanaat, H. Karcher,
A. Kreuzer, D. Lengeler, F. Pf√§fÔ¨Çe, C. Pries, W. Reichel, E. Schr√∂der,

1 Euclidean geometry
We familiarise ourselves with the axioms of Euclidean geometry in the plane
and derive some geometric implications, among them the existence of the paral-
lel line. We brieÔ¨Çy discuss the historical importance of the parallel axiom. The
existence of the Cartesian model shows the consistency of the Euclidean axioms.
The Cartesian model is used to investigate Euclidean trigonometry.
1.1
The axiomatic approach
Geometry is one of the oldest of all sciences. Remarkable geometric knowl-
edge was already present in the advanced oriental cultures of the Ô¨Åfth‚Äìthird
centuries BCE. Practical problems from metrology, architecture, astronomy
and navigation were considered on an abstract level and led to geometric laws.
For instance, the Egyptians used the formula for the area of a triangle
area = length of base line √ó height
2
and the approximate formula for the area of a circle
area =

diameter ‚àídiameter
9
2
.
The latter corresponds to an approximation of œÄ by 256
81 ‚âà3.1605. No dif-
ference was made between exact and approximate formulae in principle.
Mathematical knowledge was there in the form of laws, justiÔ¨Åcations or proofs
were not given.
This changed in Greece between 350 and 200 BCE. Aspects of usefulness
were then superseded by the desire for understanding. Mathematicians not
only wanted to know certain laws, but also why they hold. This was the starting
point of the axiomatisation of geometry. At Ô¨Årst only a few intuitively evident
axioms were laid down, from which it was thought that everything else could
be derived logically in a rigorous manner. In what follows we will become
1

2
EUCLIDEAN GEOMETRY
acquainted with the axioms of Euclidean plane geometry and go through
some rather simple implications as illustrations of the axiomatic proof. We
will mainly follow the formulation of the axioms presented by Hilbert in [13].
The axioms can be classiÔ¨Åed into Ô¨Åve groups. We begin with the incidence
axioms. To formulate these, we need two sets P and G , whose elements we
call points and straight lines respectively. Further assume that for every point
p ‚ààP and every straight line L ‚ààG the statement ‚Äúp is contained in L‚Äù, in
symbols ‚Äúp ‚ààL‚Äù, is either true or false. Note that the symbol ‚Äú‚àà‚Äù does not
denote a set-theoretic inclusion in this case, since the straight lines L are for
now not sets, but abstract elements of G . We nevertheless want to use this
suggestive notation. Let us now move on to the Ô¨Årst axioms.
Incidence axioms
These axioms make some statements about the con-
tainedness of points in straight lines.
Axiom I1
For any two points there exists a straight line that goes through both
of them,

one straight line that goes through both of them, we will from now on denote
it by L( p, q).

Every straight line contains at least two distinct points,

1.1
THE AXIOMATIC APPROACH
3
Axiomsoforder
For the formulation of these axioms we need, in addition to
the notions of P, G and ‚àà, that for every triple ( p, q, r) of points the statement
‚Äúq lies between p and r‚Äù must be either true or false. The following axioms
must be satisÔ¨Åed.
Axiom A1
If q lies between p and r, then p, q and r are three pairwise distinct
points on a straight line.
p
r
q
Axiom A2
If q lies between p and r, then q lies between r and p.
For two points p and q we call the set of all points that lie between p and q the
line segment from p to q and write pq. Axiom A2 therefore implies pr = rp.
Axiom A3
For any two distinct points p and q there exists a point r, such that
q lies between p and r.
Attention
This axiom does not say that for any two given points, there exists
another point between them. We will Ô¨Årst have to prove this, see theorem 1.1.1.
Axiom A4
Given three points, at most one of them lies between the two others.
If two straight lines L and M have a point p in common, p ‚ààL and p ‚ààM,
then we sometimes say that L and M intersect, in symbols L ‚à©M Ã∏= ‚àÖ. We say
that a line segment pr and a straight line L intersect if there exists a point q
with q ‚ààL between p and r.
Axiom A5
Let p, q and r be three points that do not lie on a straight line, let L
be a straight line that does not contain any of these three points. If L intersects
the line segment pq, then L intersects precisely one of the other two line segments
pr or qr.
p
q
r
L
This means that a straight line which enters a triangle must leave it through
one of the other two sides. It also illustrates that our geometry does not have
more than two dimensions. In three dimensions axiom A5 would not be valid:
p
q
r
L
Let us now prove a Ô¨Årst theorem, using the axioms stated so far.

4
EUCLIDEAN GEOMETRY
Theorem 1.1.1
For any two distinct points p and q there exists a point r which
lies between p and q, i.e. the line segment pq is not empty.
Proof
Let p and q be two points. By axiom I4 there exists a point s that
does not lie on the straight line L( p, q). By axiom A3 there is a point t such
that s lies between p and t. Another application of axiom A3 gives a point u
such that q lies between t and u. The straight line L := L(s, u) intersects the
line segment pt at s.
The point t does not lie on the straight line L( p, q), since otherwise s would by
axiom A1 also lie on that straight line, contradicting the choice of s. We can
therefore apply axiom A5 to the straight line L and the three points p, q and
t. As L intersects the line segment pt, L must by axiom A5 also intersect one
of the two line segments pq or tq, unless it contains one of the three points
p, q or t.
First case
L contains p or t.
Then L agrees with the straight line L( p, t) by axiom I2. Hence u lies on L( p, t)
and axiom A1 implies that q lies on L( p, t) as well. Hence p, q and t do lie on
a straight line, a contradiction.
Second case
L contains q.
Several applications of axiom I2 show that the points s, u, q, t and p lie on a
straight line, a contradiction.
Third case
L intersects the line segment tq at a point v.
p
q
t
u
s
v
L
Then L and L(t, q) have the two points u and v in common. If u = v, then
u would lie between t and q while q lies between t and u, a contradiction to
axiom A4. Thus L and L(t, q) have two distinct points in common, and axiom
I2 implies that L = L(t, q). But then both s and p lie on L, i.e. p, q and t lie on
a straight line, a contradiction.
p
q
t
u
s
r
L
Thus L and pq must intersect at a point r. In particular, pq cannot be empty.

Exercise 1.1
Let p, q, r and s be points on a straight line. Show that if q lies
between p and s and if additionally r lies between q and s, then r lies between
p and s as well.
Using this exercise and theorem 1.1.1, it is now easy to solve the following
exercise.
Exercise 1.2
Show the following: there is an inÔ¨Ånite number of distinct
points between two points.
DeÔ¨Ånition 1.1.2
Let L be a straight line, p ‚ààL. Let q and r be two points on
L, neither of which is p. We say that q and r lie on the same side of the point
p if p does not lie between q and r.
Exercise 1.3
Let L be a straight line and let p ‚ààL be a point on L. Show
that the relation ‚Äúq1 lies on the same side of p as q2‚Äù deÔ¨Ånes an equivalence
relation on the set {q ‚ààL | q Ã∏= p}.
An equivalence class of points on L that do not equal p can then be referred
to as a side of p on L.
Exercise 1.4
Show that there are exactly two sides of p on L.
DeÔ¨Ånition 1.1.3
Let L be a straight line and let p and q be two points that
do not lie on L. We say that p and q lie on the same side of a straight line L if
the line segment pq does not intersect the straight line L.
Exercise 1.5
Let L be a straight line. Show that the relation ‚Äúq1 lies on the
same side of L as q2‚Äù deÔ¨Ånes an equivalence relation on the set {q | q Ã∏‚ààL}.
Again we can call an equivalence class of points not on L a side of L.
Exercise 1.6
Show that there are exactly two sides of L.
Congruence axioms
To formulate the third group of axioms, the congru-
ence axioms, we need in addition to the previous notions that for every pair
(pq, p1q1) of line segments the formal statement ‚Äúpq is congruent to p1q1‚Äù is
either true or false.
Axiom K1 (reproduction of lengths)
Let pq be a line segment, let L1 be a
straight line, let p1, r1 ‚ààL1, r1 Ã∏= p1. Then there is a point q1 ‚ààL1 on the same
side of p1 as r1 such that pq is congruent to p1q1.

p1

6

In this axiom only the existence of a congruent line segment is required. Its
uniqueness needs to be proved later with the aid of other axioms.
Axiom K2
If the line segments p1q1 and p2q2 are both congruent to the line
segment pq, then p1q1 is congruent to p2q2 as well.
Four more congruence axioms will follow. We can nevertheless already prove
a Ô¨Årst implication.
Lemma1.1.4
The congruence of line segments deÔ¨Ånes an equivalence relation
on the set of line segments.
Proof
(a) Let pq be a line segment. We show that pq is congruent to itself.
Let L be a straight line that contains p, p ‚ààL. By axiom K1 there exists a
point r on L such that pq and pr are congruent. Then p1q1 := p2q2 := pq is
congruent to pr and hence pq is by axiom K2 congruent to itself.
(b) (symmetry) Let pq be congruent to p1q1. We show that then p1q1 is con-
gruent to pq. It follows from (a) that p1q1 is congruent to p1q1. Axiom K2 now
gives that p1q1 is congruent to pq.
(c) (transitivity) If p1q1 is congruent to p2q2 and p2q2 is congruent to p3q3,
then we need to show that p1q1 is congruent to p3q3. This follows directly
from axiom K2 together with (b).
We will from now on sometimes denote ‚Äúp1q1 is congruent to p2q2‚Äù by
‚Äúp1q1 ‚â°p2q2‚Äù.

L1. Assume that the line segments pq and qr do not have any common points,

Axiom K5 (reproduction of angles)

Axiom K5 says that we can reproduce a given angle in a unique way if we

Parallel axiom
We have seen that the existence of parallels can be proved
using the axioms introduced so far. What about their uniqueness? This is the
subject of the parallel axiom.
Axiom P ( parallel axiom)
Let L be a straight line, p a point, p Ã∏‚ààL. Then
there is at most one straight line that contains p and does not intersect L.
There has been controversy about the necessity of this axiom for thousands
of years. Many mathematicians believed that uniqueness could, as existence,
be deduced from the other axioms. There were many proof attempts. Carl
Friedrich Gauss (1777‚Äì1855) was probably the Ô¨Årst person to truly believe
in the independence of the parallel axiom. However, he never published his
views about it. The debate was ended by the Russian Nikolai Iwanowitsch
Lobatschewski (1792‚Äì1856) and by the Hungarian Janos Bolyai (1802‚Äì1860),
who, independently of each other, found a geometry that satisÔ¨Åes all axioms
except for the parallel axiom. Hence the parallel axiom cannot be derived
from the other axioms. Bolyai‚Äôs father, a teacher of mathematics himself, was
so worried about his son‚Äôs result that he wrote a letter to Gauss, asking him
for his opinion. In his answer Gauss was enthusiastic about the work of the
younger Bolyai, but added the remark that he could not praise him, since this
would be self-praise as he, Gauss, had known this for many years. Janos Bolyai
never again published work on mathematics.
The afore-mentioned geometry is hyperbolic geometry, also known as non-
Euclidean geometry. We will cover it in more detail later.

12
EUCLIDEAN GEOMETRY
Completeness axioms
When real numbers are introduced in analysis, one
becomes acquainted with the completeness axioms, which distinguish the
real numbers from the rational numbers and other ordered number Ô¨Åelds.
To determine the Euclidean geometry completely, we need the appropriate
axioms.

starting at p in direction q, contains the line segment pq.

Our geometry is therefore assumed to be non-extensible, i.e. maximal.
For a further discussion of axiomatic geometry the reader is referred to
Hilbert‚Äôs book [13]. Proofs of numerous geometric theorems, a discussion of
the uniqueness of Euclidean geometry and of the necessity of Archimedes‚Äôs
axiom, straightedge-and-compass constructions and spatial Euclidean geome-
try can be found there. On a Ô¨Ånal note, some axioms can be weakened slightly,
e.g. axioms K4 and V2.

1.2
THE CARTESIAN MODEL
13
1.2
The Cartesian model
While the axiomatic method ensures logical clarity in the structural layout
of geometry, it is also annoyingly clumsy. The proofs of even relatively simple
geometric facts can easily become quite laborious. In addition, dealing with
geometric objects that are not composed of line segments, circular lines, and
so forth is relatively involved.
We therefore now follow the ideas of the Frenchman Ren√© Descartes
(1596‚Äì1650) and characterise points by coordinates, which give the posi-
tions of the points in the plane. This allows us to use methods from algebra
and inÔ¨Ånitesimal calculus in geometry, extending our mathematical toolkit
considerably.
We hence make the deÔ¨Ånition
P := R2.
Straight lines are deÔ¨Åned as sets of points of the form
L = Lp,v := {x ‚ààR2 | x = p + t ¬∑ v, t ‚ààR},
where p, v ‚ààR2, v Ã∏= 0, are Ô¨Åxed. The set of straight lines is then
G := {Lp,v | p, v ‚ààR2, v Ã∏= 0}.
We say that a point p is contained in a straight line L if p ‚ààL in the set-
theoretic sense.

The proof of the maximality axiom V2 requires more thought than those of

Theorem 1.2.3
With the deÔ¨Ånitions made above the maximality axiom V2 is
valid.
Proof
Let P‚Ä≤ ‚äÉP and G ‚Ä≤ ‚äÉG be respectively the set of points and the set
of straight lines of an extension of our Cartesian model of Euclidean geometry.
We will call the points from P old points, and those from P‚Ä≤ ‚àíP new points.
We use analogous notation when dealing with straight lines. It needs to be

1.2
THE CARTESIAN MODEL
15
shown that there cannot be new points and straight lines at all, P = P‚Ä≤ and
G = G ‚Ä≤.
The proof is accomplished in three steps. We Ô¨Årst show that the old straight
lines do not contain new points, then that there are no new points, and Ô¨Ånally
that there are no new straight lines.
(a) Old straight lines do not contain new points, i.e. for L ‚ààG and p ‚ààP‚Ä≤ with
p ‚àà‚Ä≤ L we have p ‚ààP.
One must not become confused at this point. According to our deÔ¨Ånition,
old straight lines are sets of old points. In the set-theoretic sense old straight
lines only contain old points anyway. Nevertheless, it is a priori possible
that new points are contained in old straight lines for the extension ‚àà‚Ä≤ of the
set-theoretic inclusion ‚àà. It now needs to be shown that this is not the case.
For this purpose let L ‚ààG be an old straight line. Suppose that L contains
a new point n. We choose an old point p1 ‚ààL. As L is of the form L = Lp,v,
we can write p1 = p + t1v =: c(t1), t1 ‚ààR. If we reproduce the line seg-
ment (0, 0)‚ä§(0, 1)‚ä§sufÔ¨Åciently often on L, starting at p1 in direction n, then we
obtain by Archimedes‚Äôs axiom a second point q1 ‚ààL such that n lies between
p1 and q1. Reproducing the line segment (0, 0)‚ä§(0, 1)‚ä§on an old straight line
starting at an old point always gives another old point. Thus q1 is an old point.
We write q1 = c(s1), s1 ‚ààR. Without loss of generality let t1 < s1.
We now decompose the real numbers into two disjoint subsets, R = T ‚äîS,
where T = {t ‚ààR | c(t) lies on the same side of n as c(t1)} and S = {t ‚àà
R | c(t) lies on the same side of n as c(s1)}. This decomposition of the real
numbers constitutes a Dedekind cut. By the completeness of R either the sub-
set T must have a maximum or S a minimum. We deal with the case that T has
a maximum t2. The other case can be treated analogously.
We set p2 := c(t2). As t2 ‚ààT, the points p1 and p2 must lie on the same
side of n. By Archimedes‚Äôs axiom, there is a natural number k such that k-fold
reproduction of the line segment p2n on L from p2 in direction q1 contains
the line segment p2q1. We set p3 := c(t3) with t3 = t2 + (s1 ‚àít2)/k. Then the
line segment p2p3 has the property that its k-fold reproduction on L from p2
in direction q1 gives the line segment p2q1.

(b) There are no new points, i.e. P = P‚Ä≤.

The axioms of Euclidean plane geometry are thus valid for the Cartesian
model. In particular, we see that the axioms are consistent.
Doing geometry in the Cartesian model has the advantage that we now
have the whole mathematical machinery of differential and integral calcu-
lus to hand. This makes the treatment of Euclidean trigonometry relatively
easy.

For comparison, we conclude this section by looking at another important clas-
sical geometry, i.e. spherical geometry. The points of this geometry are not in
the plane, but on the surface of a sphere. We set
P := S2 := {(x, y, z)‚ä§‚ààR3 | x2 + y2 + z2 = 1}.
We call S2 the two-dimensional sphere.
This raises the question of what the ‚Äústraight lines‚Äù are in this geometry.
The straight lines in the plane can be characterised by the fact that each of
them is the shortest curve connecting any two of its points. On the sphere the
great circles have this property. A great circle is the subset of S2 in which
a two-dimensional vector subspace of R3, i.e. a plane that passes through 0,
intersects the surface of the sphere.
We therefore set
G := {S2 ‚à©E | E is a two-dimensional vector subspace of R3}.
The incidence relation ‚ààmust here be interpreted in the set-theoretic sense
again.

Given three points on a great circle, it is not possible to say in a meaningful
way which of them lies between the two others. Let us try with the following

deÔ¨Ånition: if p, q, r ‚ààS2, then q lies between p and r if and only if p, q and r are

The congruence relations on the other hand can easily be deÔ¨Åned in a geomet-
rically meaningful way. We simply exchange the Euclidean motion group E(2)
for the orthogonal group O(3). One needs to pay attention to the fact that for
an orthogonal matrix A ‚ààO(3) and p ‚ààS2 the point Ap should lie on S2 again,
i.e. A indeed maps S2 to itself only.
Exercise 1.20
Show that axioms K1‚ÄìK6 are valid.
There are no parallels since any two great circles intersect. Theorem 1.1.9
therefore does not hold. This is one of the ways to see that not all incidence
and ordering axioms can hold.
Because of its importance in navigation, amony other things, spherical
geometry has been studied for a very long time. We will investigate it in more
detail in sections 4.9 and 4.10.

2 Curve theory
We analyse curves in n-dimensional space with a special focus on plane curves
and space curves. Length, curvature and torsion are introduced. We prove
Hopf‚Äôs Umlaufsatz for simple closed curves, characterise convex curves and
derive the four-vertex theorem. The isoperimetric inequality, which compares
the length of a simple closed plane curve with the enclosed area, is proved using
the Fourier series. We show that for a given curvature and torsion the resulting
space curve is unique up to a Euclidean motion. We investigate how much a
space curve needs to curve if it is closed and make the result even stronger in the
case that the space curve is knotted.
2.1
Curves in Rn
We now want to use the tools of differentiation and integration to describe
curves in n-dimensional space. We usually graphically imagine a curve as a
bent line in space. Mathematically we express this as follows:
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.1
Let I ‚äÇR be an interval. A parametrised curve is a map
c : I ‚ÜíRn that can be differentiated inÔ¨Ånitely often. A parametrised curve is
said to be regular if its velocity vector does not vanish anywhere; Àôc(t) Ã∏= 0 for
all t ‚ààI.
The interval I from the deÔ¨Ånition may be open, closed or half-open; further-
more, I can be bounded or unbounded. The condition Àôc(t) Ã∏= 0 ensures that
the point c(t) on the curve moves at t ‚ààI. In particular, this excludes the con-
stant map c(t) = c0. This certainly makes sense, as the image of this map only
consists of the point c0; not exactly what we have in mind when thinking of a
curve. Let us look at some examples.

DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.8
A parameter transformation œï is called orientation-
preserving if Àôœï(t) > 0 for all t. The parameter transformation œï is said to be
orientation-reversing if Àôœï(t) < 0 for all t.
Every parameter transformation is either orientation-preserving or orientation-
reversing. This can easily be seen as follows: suppose that there exists a t1 ‚ààI
with Àôœï(t1) < 0 and a t2 ‚ààI with Àôœï(t2) > 0, then by the intermediate value
theorem there would also exist a t3 between t1 and t2 with Àôœï(t3) = 0. But this
is impossible, as we have seen before.
We imagine a curve as a parametrised curve, while the actual choice of
parametrisation is regarded as irrelevant. This is made more precise through
the following:
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.9
A curve is an equivalence class of regular parametrised
curves, where those curves that are reparametrisations of each other are
regarded as equivalent.
The regular parametrised curves from examples 2.1.2‚Äì2.1.6 are different
curves since they have distinct images in Rn and hence cannot be obtained
from each other by reparametrisations.
The regular parametrised curves
c1 : R ‚ÜíR2,
c1(t) = (t, t)
and
c2 : (0, ‚àû) ‚ÜíR2,
c2(t) = (ln t, ln t),
however, are equivalent, since c1 = c2 ‚ó¶œï with œï(t) = e t, and hence represent
the same curve.
If a curve is represented by a regular parametrised curve c : I ‚ÜíRn, then
the image c(I) is said to be the trace of the curve.

2.1
CURVES IN Rn
27
Curves do not have an intrinsic orientation, since it can be reversed by a
parameter transformation. To be able to deÔ¨Åne an orientation we make use of
the following deÔ¨Ånition:
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.10
An oriented curve is an equivalence class of parametrised
curves, where those that originate from each other through orientation-
preserving reparametrisations are regarded as equivalent.
Every oriented curve determines exactly one curve. Every curve has exactly
two orientations, i.e. there are exactly two oriented curves that determine a
given curve.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.11
A unit speed curve or curve parametrised by arc-length is
a regular parametrised curve c : I ‚ÜíRn with ‚à•Àôc(t)‚à•= 1 for all t ‚ààI.
Curves parametrised by arc-length are exactly those that traverse their image
in Rn with constant velocity 1. Slightly more generally we also say:
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.12
A curve parametrised proportional to arc-length is a
regular parametrised curve c : I ‚ÜíRn, for which ‚à•Àôc‚à•is constant (but not
necessarily equal to 1).
Curves parametrised by arc-length are for many purposes particularly conve-
nient. But do they exist?

Lemma 2.1.16 says that we can talk about the length of curves, not only of the
length of parametrised curves. The length does not depend on the particular
parametrisation. Indeed, the length of a road does not depend on the speed at
which you have driven along it.

DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.19
A parametrised curve c : R ‚ÜíRn is called periodic with
period L if for all t ‚ààR we have c(t + L) = c(t), L > 0, and there is no
0 < L‚Ä≤ < L such that c(t + L‚Ä≤) = c(t) for all t ‚ààR as well. A curve is called
closed if it has a periodic regular parametrisation.
The circular line from example 2.1.3 is a periodic parametrised curve with
period L = 2œÄ. Hence the curve represented by this parametrisation is closed.
Not every parametrisation of a closed curve is periodic. One can, for exam-
ple, reparametrise a periodic parametrised curve in such a way that it becomes

2.1
CURVES IN Rn
33
slower during each traverse of the curve. Then the time interval needed for
the traverse will increase. This reparametrisation is then no longer periodic.
However, the statement in the following exercise holds.
Exercise 2.4
Show that if c : R ‚ÜíRn is a parametrisation by arc-length of
a closed curve, then c is periodic.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.20
A closed curve is a simple closed curve if it has a periodic
regular parametrisation c with period L such that c|[0,L) is injective.
a simple closed curve
a closed curve, but
not simple closed
This condition says that the curve does not have any self-intersections, apart
from the point where it ‚Äúcloses itself‚Äù. As parameter transformations are bijec-
tive, the injectivity condition holds not only for one periodic parametrisation
of the closed curve, but automatically for all of its periodic parametrisations.
The choice of the periodic parametrisation of the closed curve does not play a
role in determining whether it is a simple closed curve or not.

The number œë(t) measures the angle between the velocity vector Àôc(t) and the

smooth function of t. Of course, one could uniquely determine the angle by

jump discontinuities at those points where the velocity vector has completed
one turn.

The lemma shows that the winding number of an oriented closed plane curve is

is changed.
Further we can establish that the winding number is always a whole number.

The reader may already expect there to be a connection between winding
number and curvature. If a curve curves long enough to the left (Œ∫ > 0), then
it should at some point complete a winding and should make a positive contri-
bution to the winding number. Correspondingly curvature to the right (Œ∫ < 0)
should make a negative contribution to the winding number. This is formalised
in the following theorem.

A priori one could for a given curve need condition (2.5) for some t0 and
(2.6) for other t0. In reality this does not occur. This is because if a convex
curve satisÔ¨Åes ‚ü®c(t) ‚àíc(t1), n(t1)‚ü©‚â•0 for all t and ‚ü®c(t) ‚àíc(t2), n(t2)‚ü©‚â§0 for
all t, then continuity implies that there exists a t3 between t1 and t2 such that
‚ü®c(t) ‚àíc(t3), n(t3)‚ü©= 0 for all t. This implies that c is a straight line and thus
both (2.5) and (2.6) are satisÔ¨Åed for all t0. We have proved:
Lemma 2.2.14
Let c : I ‚ÜíR2 be a plane curve parametrised by arc-length
with normal Ô¨Åeld n. Then c is convex if and only if for all t, t0 ‚ààI
‚ü®c(t) ‚àíc(t0), n(t0)‚ü©‚â•0
or for all t, t0 ‚ààI
‚ü®c(t) ‚àíc(t0), n(t0)‚ü©‚â§0.
Intuitively it seems clear that a convex curve always curves in the same sense,
i.e. always to the left or always to the right. This is formalised in the following
theorem.

Remark
The following example shows that it is indeed important for the
validity of this theorem that the curve is assumed to be simple closed.
Œ∫ > 0,
but not convex

that convexity follows from Œ∫ ‚â•0. We do not need to make this assumption

For a parametrisation by arc-length of a convex (but not necessarily closed) curve
we have Œ∫(t) ‚â•0 for all t or Œ∫(t) ‚â§0 for all t.

First case

Lemma 2.2.20
If a simple closed convex plane curve intersects a straight line
at more than one point tangentially, then the curve contains a segment of some
straight line.
Proof of the lemma
If the curve has more than two points of intersection
with the straight line G, then the claim follows from lemma 2.2.19. Hence we
can assume that the curve intersects the straight line G at exactly two points.
Because of the convexity of the curve it must lie entirely on one side of the
straight line. We shift the straight line by a sufÔ¨Åciently small distance towards
the curve and obtain a parallel straight line G‚Ä≤. Continuity implies that G‚Ä≤ must
intersect the curve in at least two points in the vicinity of each of the two points
of intersection with G.
G
G‚Ä≤
Hence G‚Ä≤ has at least four points of intersection with the curve and because of
lemma 2.2.19 the curve contains a segment of the straight line G‚Ä≤.
In lemma 2.2.20 it is not claimed that the curve contains a segment of the tan-
gentially intersecting straight line (although this is also true), but the segment
contained in the curve may by all means be part of another straight line. How-
ever, for our application of the lemma in the proof of the four-vertex theorem
this does not play a role.

To conclude this section about plane curves we consider the following opti-
mization problem, whose agricultural formulation could read as follows:
suppose a farmer has a given length of fence, e.g. 10 km. What would the
largest paddock that the farmer could fence look like?
The following theorem gives us the answer: the paddock would be
circular.
Theorem 2.2.21 (isoperimetric inequality)
Let G ‚äÇR2 be a bounded region with
the simple closed plain curve c as boundary. Let A[G] be the area of the surface.

2.3
Space curves
We will now consider those curves that run through three-dimensional space,
i.e. take values in R3.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.1
A parametrised curve c : I ‚ÜíR3 is called a parametrised
space curve. Regular parametrised space curves, space curves and oriented
space curves are deÔ¨Åned analogously.
Unlike for plane curves it is now not straightforward to deÔ¨Åne a normal Ô¨Åeld.
If c : I ‚ÜíR3 is a space curve parametrised by arc-length, then the vec-
tors perpendicular to the velocity vector Àôc(t) form a plane, the unit vectors
perpendicular to the velocity vector form a circle.
Àôc(t)
c
In the case of plane curves there were two perpendicular unit vectors. We
used this fact to deÔ¨Åne the normal vector. Which normal vector should we
choose in the case of space curves? The deÔ¨Ånition of curvature is a priori also
a problem, since it required the normal vector. Let us recall that the sign of
the curvature described whether a particular curve curves to the left or to the
right. What should it mean in the context of space curves?
However, there is a way to avoid this problem. Let us recall that for plane
curves
¬®c(t) = Œ∫(t) ¬∑ n(t).
(2.14)
It follows that
|Œ∫(t)| = ‚à•¬®c(t)‚à•.
If we give up the sign of the curvature, then we can deÔ¨Åne it without reference
to the normal Ô¨Åeld. We will therefore deÔ¨Åne the curvature of a space curve as
follows.

58
CURVE THEORY
DeÔ¨Ånition2.3.2
Let c : I ‚ÜíR3 be a space curve parametrised by arc-length.
The function Œ∫ : I ‚ÜíR, Œ∫(t) := ‚à•¬®c(t)‚à•, is called the curvature of c.
Again curvature is a measure of how much a curve deviates from a straight
line. Concretely, if c is a space curve parametrised by arc-length, then c is a
straight line if and only if ¬®c ‚â°0, i.e. if Œ∫ ‚â°0. But now the curvature is always
‚â•0. It no longer makes sense to talk about a curve curving to the left or to the
right.
Attention
We can also regard plane curves as space curves, since the plane is
contained in three-dimensional space, e.g. as the x‚Äìy plane. We therefore now
have two different deÔ¨Ånitions for the curvature of plane curves. If Àúc : I ‚ÜíR2
is a plane curve parametrised by arc-length and with curvature ÀúŒ∫ : I ‚ÜíR and
c = (Àúc, 0) : I ‚ÜíR3 is the same parametrised curve, considered as a space curve
with curvature Œ∫ : I ‚ÜíR, then
Œ∫(t) = ‚à•¬®c(t)‚à•= ‚à•(¬®Àúc(t), 0)‚à•= ‚à•¬®Àúc(t)‚à•= |ÀúŒ∫(t)|.
Knowing what the curvature of a space curve is, we can now deÔ¨Åne the normal
Ô¨Åeld using (2.14). However, this will only work if the curvature does not vanish.

Let t0 ‚ààI and Œ∫(t0) Ã∏= 0. Then

DeÔ¨Ånition2.3.4
Let c : I ‚ÜíR3 be a space curve parametrised by arc-length.

Here √ó denotes the cross product in R3. The vector product x √ó y of two

that x, y, x √ó y form a positively oriented basis of R3 if x and y are linearly

positively oriented orthonormal basis.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.5
The orthonormal basis (Àôc(t0), n(t0), b(t0)) is called the
Frenet dreibein or Frenet trihedron of c at t0.

Attention
The Frenet dreibein is only deÔ¨Åned for those t for which Œ∫(t) Ã∏= 0.
The curvature of a plane curve says how much the velocity vector turns in
the direction of the normal vector. We can introduce a similar notion for a
space curve. It will measure how much the normal vector turns out of the
plane spanned by itself and the velocity vector, i.e. how much it moves in the
direction of the binormal vector.
DeÔ¨Ånition2.3.6

is called the torsion of c at t0.

Exercise 2.16
Show that the curvature and the torsion of a space curve are
invariant under orientation-preserving Euclidean motions. More precisely, if
c : I ‚ÜíR3 is a space curve parametrised by arc-length with curvature Œ∫ > 0
and if F ‚ààE(3) is an orientation-preserving Euclidean motion, i.e. F(x) =

Àúc := F ‚ó¶c satisfy
ÀúŒ∫ = Œ∫, ÀúœÑ = œÑ.
What happens if the Euclidean motion is orientation-reversing?
We will now see that one can Ô¨Ånd a space curve if curvature and torsion are
given. According to exercise 2.16 this space curve can at most be unique up to
Euclidean motions. Indeed, this is the only ambiguity.
Theorem 2.3.9 (fundamental theorem of space curve theory)

Remark
The parametrisation by arc-length of a closed space curve is peri-
odic and unique up to parameter transformations of the form t ‚Üí¬±t + t0. Such
parameter transformations do not change the value of the total curvature. We
can therefore not only talk about the total curvature of a parametrised closed
space curve, but also about the total curvature of a closed space curve.
If c is a periodic space curve parametrised by arc-length and with period L,
then we say that the polygon P = (a0, . . . , am‚àí1) is inscribed in c, if there exists
a partition 0 ‚â§t1 < t2 < ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ < tm < L with ai = c(ti).
We have already met inscribed polygons in the context of lengths of curves
in proposition 2.1.18. Like there we see that the total curvature of inscribed
polygons approximates the total curvature of a closed space curve.

As a very pleasing application of the concept of the bridge number of space
curves we obtain Fenchel‚Äôs theorem, which states how much a space curve
needs to curve in order to become a closed curve.

CURVE THEORY
We will conclude this chapter with a treatment of the concept of knotted
curves and an investigation into how much a space curve needs to curve so
that it can be knotted.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.20
An isotopy of R3 is a continuous map  : [0, 1]√óR3 ‚ÜíR3
such that for every Ô¨Åxed t ‚àà[0, 1] the map (t, ¬∑) : R3 ‚ÜíR3 is a homeo-
morphism. Two simple closed space curves c0 and c1 are called ambient
isotopic, if there exists an isotopy  of R3 with (0, x) = x for all x ‚ààR3
and (1, Trace(c0)) = Trace(c1).
We graphically interpret t ‚àà[0, 1] as a deformation parameter.  bends the
curve c0 into the curve c1. The word ‚Äúambient‚Äù in the deÔ¨Ånition suggests that
not only the curves are deformed: the homeomorphism (t, ¬∑) always deforms
the entire surrounding R3.
Exercise 2.19
Show that ambient isotopy deÔ¨Ånes an equivalence relation on
the set of simple closed space curves.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.21
An ambient isotopy class of simple closed space curves is
a knot. A simple closed space curve is said to be unknotted if it is ambient
isotopic to a simple closed plane curve. Otherwise it is called knotted.
So a simple closed space curve is unknotted if it can be bent into a sim-
ple closed plane curve without the curve intersecting itself in the process of
deformation. The following curve for instance is unknotted:
The so-called trefoil is knotted. Readers can convince themselves of this using
a piece of rope (at this point the mathematical proof for this would be too
involved).
For a curve to be knotted it probably needs to curve even more than in
Fenchel‚Äôs theorem. Indeed the following holds:
Theorem 2.3.22 (F¬¥ary‚ÄìMilnor theorem)

John Milnor and Istv√°n F√°ry discovered this theorem at roughly the same
time independently of each other, see [11] and [21]. As a young student
John Milnor attended a course on differential geometry taught by Al Tucker
in 1948 in Princeton. Milnor managed to solve a conjecture of Karol Borsuk
from a collection of open problems presented in class. This problem was noth-
ing other than our theorem 2.3.22. According to an anecdote Milnor came
late to a lecture and mistook the problems for (unusually difÔ¨Åcult) homework
exercises.

3 Classical surface theory
We introduce regular surfaces and their tangent planes. We investigate what
normal Ô¨Åelds have to do with orientability of surfaces. We discover that the
geometry of regular surfaces is largely determined by the Ô¨Årst and second funda-
mental forms, which also give rise to different types of curvature. We learn how
to integrate over regular surfaces and, in particular, how their surface area is
deÔ¨Åned. Finally we examine some special classes of surfaces more closely: ruled
surfaces, minimal surfaces, surfaces of revolution and tubular surfaces.
3.1
Regular surfaces
Surfaces in three-dimensional space are two-dimensional objects, i.e. the
points on a surface can be described by two independent parameters. The
deÔ¨Ånition which follows is local. Unlike with curves, which we always
parametrised as a whole, we only require that small parts of the surface can
be described by a parametrisation.
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.1.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a subset. We call S a regular surface if there
exists for every point p ‚ààS an open neighbourhood V of p in R3, and if, in
addition, there exists an open subset U ‚äÇR2 and a smooth map F : U ‚ÜíR3
such that
(i)
F(U) = S ‚à©V and F : U ‚ÜíS ‚à©V is a homeomorphism and
(ii)
the Jacobian DuF has rank 2 for every point u ‚ààU.

3.2
The tangent plane
The simplest regular surfaces are certainly planes, just as straight lines are the
simplest curves. We would therefore now like to approximate possibly very
complicated surfaces by planes. This concept is very similar to the differential
of a smooth map. For a smooth map F : U ‚ÜíRm, U ‚äÇRn open and p ‚ààU,
the map Rn ‚ÜíRm, x ‚ÜíF( p) + DpF(x ‚àíp) is the Ô¨Årst (the afÔ¨Åne linear)
approximation at the point p. What is now the geometric equivalent to DpF
for a regular surface?
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.2.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface, let p ‚ààS. Then
TpS = {X ‚ààR3 | there exists an Œµ > 0 and a smooth parametrised
curve c : (‚àíŒµ, Œµ) ‚ÜíS with c(0) = p and Àôc(0) = X}
is called the tangent plane of S in p. The elements of the tangent plane are
called tangent vectors.

obtained by a translation of TpS to the root point p.

3.3
The Ô¨Årst fundamental form
In order to do geometry on a regular surface S ‚äÇR3, we need to be able
to measure, for example, lengths of curves that lie in S or the angle between
two tangent vectors to the surface. Since the tangent plane at each point p ‚ààS
is a two-dimensional subspace of R3, we can restrict the conventional scalar
product ‚ü®¬∑, ¬∑‚ü©of R3 to TpS, and obtain a Euclidean scalar product on TpS. The
map, which assigns each point p ‚ààS this restriction gp := ‚ü®¬∑, ¬∑‚ü©|TpS√óTpS, is called
Ô¨Årst fundamental form of S. We often write the Ô¨Årst fundamental form as
Ip(X, Y) = gp(X, Y) = ‚ü®X, Y‚ü©,
where X, Y ‚ààTpS.
As learnt in linear algebra, every Euclidean scalar product on a vector
space, here gp on TpS, can be represented as a positive deÔ¨Ånite symmetric
matrix after a choice of basis. A basis of TpS is usually obtained by a local
parametrisation (U, F, V) of S at p. If e1, e2 are the standard basis vectors of
R2, then DuF(e1) = (‚àÇF/‚àÇu1)(u) and DuF(e2) = (‚àÇF/‚àÇu2)(u), u = F‚àí1( p), form
a basis of TpS. With respect to this basis, the matrix representation of gp is then
given by
gij(u) := gp(DuF(ei), DuF(ej)) =
 ‚àÇF
‚àÇui (u), ‚àÇF
‚àÇu j (u)

The 2√ó2 matrix (gij(u))i,j=1,2 is therefore symmetric and positive deÔ¨Ånite. Fur-
ther, the above formula shows immediately that the matrix entries gij depend
smoothly on u, i.e. gij : U ‚ÜíR is a smooth function for every i and j.

3.4
Normal Ô¨Åelds and orientability
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.4.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface. A normal Ô¨Åeld on S is a
map
N : S ‚ÜíR3,
such that N( p)‚ä•TpS for all p ‚ààS. A normal Ô¨Åeld on S is said to be a unit
normal Ô¨Åeld, if in addition ‚à•N( p)‚à•= 1 for all p ‚ààS.
Remark
Note that if N is a (unit) normal Ô¨Åeld on S, then the same is true
for ‚àíN. Continuous unit normal Ô¨Åelds may, but do not necessarily, exist on
regular surfaces.
Example 3.4.2

Example 3.4.5
Let S be the M√∂bius strip. It is obtained by gluing together
a strip of paper at its left and its right end, but, in order not to obtain the
cylindrical surface, twisting it once.
The M√∂bius strip does not have a continuous unit normal Ô¨Åeld.
?
This also means that the M√∂bius strip only has one side, there is no ‚Äúinside‚Äù or
‚Äúoutside‚Äù. If one starts colouring the M√∂bius strip at one point, then one will
Ô¨Ånd after a while that the strip is coloured everywhere on both sides.
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.4.6
A regular surface S ‚äÇR3 is orientable if there exists a
smooth unit normal Ô¨Åeld on S.
Remark
Hence the plane, the sphere and the cylinder are orientable, while
the M√∂bius strip is not. The main condition in the deÔ¨Ånition is that the
unit normal Ô¨Åeld is smooth. It is always possible to Ô¨Ånd some unit normal
Ô¨Åeld by choosing for each point p ‚ààS one of the two unit normal vectors to
TpS ‚äÇR3 and calling it N( p). But this N will usually not be continuous, let

Theorem 3.4.7
A regular surface S ‚äÇR3 is orientable if and only if S can be

3.5
The second fundamental form
Let S ‚äÇR3 be an orientable regular surface with smooth unit normal Ô¨Åeld N.
Regarded as a map between surfaces, i.e. N : S ‚ÜíS2, the map N is also called
the Gauss map.
Let p ‚ààS. We consider the differential of N at p:
dpN : TpS ‚ÜíTN( p)S2.
Now TN( p)S2 = N( p)‚ä•= TpS. Hence dpN is an endomorphism on TpS.
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.5.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface with orientation given by
the unit normal Ô¨Åeld N. The endomorphism
Wp : TpS ‚ÜíTpS,
Wp(X) = ‚àídpN(X),
is called the Weingarten map.
The negative sign appears for historic reasons. If orientation is reversed, i.e.
if ‚àíN is substituted for N, then W also changes its sign.
Example 3.5.2
Let S = S2, and let N be the outer unit normal Ô¨Åeld,
N( p) = p. Then
Wp = ‚àíId : TpS2 ‚ÜíTpS2.
Example 3.5.3
Let S = {(x, y, 0)‚ä§| x, y ‚ààR} be the x‚Äìy plane, N(x, y, z) =
(0, 0, 1)‚ä§. Then N is constant and thus Wp = 0 for all p ‚ààS.

Curvature
We now come to a central notion of surface theory and of differential geome-
try: curvature. We will meet several concepts of curvature, and we begin with
normal curvature.
For this purpose let S ‚äÇR3 be an orientable regular surface with smooth
unit normal Ô¨Åeld N, p ‚ààS. Let c : (‚àíŒµ, Œµ) ‚ÜíS be a curve parametrised by arc-

0 curvature Œ∫(0), which in the case Œ∫(0) Ã∏= 0 is given by
¬®c(0) = Œ∫(0) ¬∑ n(0),
where n is the normal vector of c. We now want to split this curvature into a
part which results from the fact that c curves within S, and a part which reÔ¨Çects
the curvature of S in R3. For this purpose we decompose n(0) into a part that

Theorem 3.6.1 (Meusnier‚Äôs theorem)

Œ∫nor = II(Àôc(0), Àôc(0)).
In particular, all curves parametrised by arc-length in S through p with the same
tangent vector have the same normal curvature.

DeÔ¨Ånition 3.6.3
The eigenvalues Œ∫1 and Œ∫2 are called the principal curva-
tures of S at the point p. The corresponding eigenvectors ¬±X1 and ¬±X2 are
called principal curvature directions.
Unless stated otherwise, we use the convention Œ∫1 ‚â§Œ∫2. An arbitrary unit
vector X ‚ààTpS can be expressed in terms of the basis X1, X2 by
X = cos(œï) ¬∑ X1 + sin(œï) ¬∑ X2

II(X, X) = cos2(œï) ¬∑ Œ∫1 + sin2(œï) ¬∑ Œ∫2.
In particular, we observe that Œ∫1 and Œ∫2 are the minimum and maximum of all
normal curvatures of S at p, if X runs through all directions, i.e. for all unit
vectors X ‚ààTpS.

DeÔ¨Ånition 3.6.9
Let S ‚äÇR3 be an oriented regular surface, let p ‚ààS be a
point. Let Œ∫1 and Œ∫2 be the principal curvatures of S at p. Then
K( p) := Œ∫1 ¬∑ Œ∫2 = det(Wp)
is the Gauss curvature of S at p. Further,
H( p) := Œ∫1 + Œ∫2
2
= 1
2trace(Wp)
is called the mean curvature of S at p.
Both concepts of curvature represent an average of the two principal curva-
tures: the mean curvature is the arithmetic mean, the Gauss curvature is the
square of the geometric mean. We will investigate the geometric meaning of
these notions of curvature later.

116
CLASSICAL SURFACE THEORY
Reversing the orientation of an oriented regular surface changes the signs of
the principal curvatures. Hence H( p) changes to ‚àíH( p), while K( p) remains
unchanged. Hence the Gauss curvature is also deÔ¨Åned for non-orientable sur-
faces, while the mean curvature is only deÔ¨Åned up to the sign. In order to
obtain a version of the mean curvature that does not vary with orientation
and that is deÔ¨Åned even on non-orientable surfaces, we often consider not the
real-valued function H but the mean curvature Ô¨Åeld H , which is deÔ¨Åned by
H := H ¬∑ N.
(3.6)
Unlike the mean curvature H, H is not a function, but a normal Ô¨Åeld on the
surface.
Now some more terminology.
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.6.10

(ii)

(iii)
parabolic, if K( p) = 0, but Wp Ã∏= 0, i.e. if one of the two principal curvatures
vanishes while the other does not,
(iv)
planar, if Wp = 0, i.e. Œ∫1 = Œ∫2 = 0.
Remark

also make sense on non-orientable surfaces, although in this case Wp is only
deÔ¨Åned up to a change of sign.

CLASSICAL SURFACE THEORY
We now want to look at special local parametrisations of regular surfaces
that show us that the second fundamental form approximately tells us how
the surface moves in the proximity of a point away from the tangent plane. In
particular, we will see that every surface can be given locally as a graph on the
tangent plane. We will then investigate what curvatures tell us about the local
geometric behaviour of the surface.
Theorem 3.6.15

CLASSICAL SURFACE THEORY
Corollary 3.6.18
Let S be a compact regular surface, let q ‚ààR3, let p ‚ààS be a
point on S with minimal distance from q. Then q ‚àíp is perpendicular to TpS.
We will later generalise the concept of the Ô¨Årst fundamental form and move
on to surfaces with Riemannian metrics. Those surfaces also have a Gauss
curvature. It will then be possible to Ô¨Ånd compact surfaces with K ‚â§0, even
K ‚â°‚àí1 is possible. Theorem 3.6.17 should be understood as follows: com-
pact surfaces with K ‚â§0 do exist in an abstract sense that remains to be made
precise, but they cannot be embedded in R3 without bending it somewhere to
such an extent that Gauss curvature becomes positive.

How do we know whether a function f can be written in the form (3.9)? Simply
writing the function as a Ô¨Ånite sum f = f1 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ + fk, where each fi vanishes

3.8
Some classes of surfaces
We now want to study some special classes of surfaces. These are the ruled
surfaces, which consist of straight lines, surfaces of revolution, which result
from a rotation of a plane curve around an axis, tubular surfaces, which are
deÔ¨Åned by space curves, as well as minimal surfaces, which are important in
the theory of soap Ô¨Ålms.
3.8.1 Ruled
surfaces
Let I ‚äÇR be an open interval and let c : I ‚ÜíR3 be a parametrised space
curve. We now want to attach a straight line to each point of this curve, which
will give us a surface. For this purpose let v : I ‚ÜíR3 be a smooth map with
v(t) Ã∏= (0, 0, 0)‚ä§for all t ‚ààI. Let J ‚äÇR be another open interval. We set

F(t, s) = c(t) + sv(t).
(3.10)
c(t)

To check whether this gives us a regular surface we Ô¨Ånd the differential of F:
D(t,s)F = (Àôc(t) + sÀôv(t), v(t)).
If we require that v(t) and Àôc(t) are linearly independent, then the matrix D(t,0)F
has full rank for a Ô¨Åxed t. Hence there exists an open neighbourhood of
(t, 0) in I √ó J such that (U, F, S) is a parametrisation of the regular surface
S = F(U).
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.8.1
A regular surface S ‚äÇR3 which can be covered by para-

If we want to Ô¨Ånd a surface S that is enclosed by a closed space curve c, ‚àÇS = c,
and has minimal surface area, then this surface necessarily needs to satisfy
H ‚â°(0, 0, 0)‚ä§. This leads to the following deÔ¨Ånition.
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.8.10
A regular surface S ‚äÇR3 is called a minimal surface if
H ‚â°(0, 0, 0)‚ä§.
Note that minimal surfaces do not necessarily minimise area. H ‚â°(0, 0, 0)‚ä§is
only a necessary condition.
The question of whether we can, for a given closed space curve c, Ô¨Ånd a sur-
face of minimum area with boundary c is known as the Plateau‚Äôs problem. The
answer is ‚Äúyes‚Äù for a very general class of boundary curves, see, for example,
[1] for a discussion of this problem. We can physically create minimal surfaces
by dipping a closed wire (our closed space curve) into a soap solution. The
soap Ô¨Ålm created is then a minimal surface.
Remark
If the surface S is orientable, then there exists a smooth unit nor-
mal Ô¨Åeld N on S and we can write the mean normal curvature Ô¨Åeld in the form
H = H ¬∑ N. The minimum area condition is then
H ‚â°0.

Theorem 3.8.15
For every regular surface we have
K ‚â§H2.
In particular, the Gauss curvature of minimal surfaces satisÔ¨Åes
K ‚â§0.
Proof
Expressing the mean curvature and the Gauss curvature in terms of
the principal curvatures, H = (Œ∫1 + Œ∫2)/2, K = Œ∫1Œ∫2, we observe that
4 ¬∑ (H2 ‚àíK) = (Œ∫1 + Œ∫2)2 ‚àí4 ¬∑ Œ∫1Œ∫2 = (Œ∫1 ‚àíŒ∫2)2 ‚â•0.
The claim follows.
Corollary 3.8.16
Compact minimal surfaces do not exist.
Proof
By theorem 3.6.17 every compact surface S ‚äÇR3 has a point with
positive Gauss curvature. By theorem 3.8.15 it follows that S cannot be a
minimal surface.

4 The inner geometry of surfaces
We use the notion of an isometry to make the concept of inner geometry of sur-
faces more precise. Vector Ô¨Åelds and their Ô¨Årst and second covariant derivatives
are introduced. The Theorema Egregrium (‚Äòremarkable theorem‚Äô) expresses the
Gauss curvature in terms of the curvature tensor and shows the Gauss curva-
ture belongs to the inner geometry of the surface. General Riemann metrics
generalise the Ô¨Årst fundamental form. The problem of the shortest way from
one point to another leads to the concept of the geodesic and the Riemann
exponential mapping. In this way it is particularly straightforward to obtain
coordinates that are convenient in geometry, like Riemann normal coordinates,
geodesic polar coordinates and Fermi coordinates. Jacobi Ô¨Åelds illustrate the
inner geometric importance of the Gauss curvature. Spherical and hyperbolic
geometry are investigated in more detail. Their trigonometry is derived and
applications to cartography are discussed. The hyperbolic plane satisÔ¨Åes all
axioms of Euclidean geometry except for the parallel axiom.
4.1
Isometries
When we consider surfaces in R3 we tend to pay special attention to their rel-
ative geometries, i.e. to how the surface is embedded into the surrounding
space. We quasi look at them from outside. One could also try to imagine
oneself in the position of a (two-dimensional) inhabitant of the surface, and
examine those properties of the surface that can be observed by a being who
cannot peek out of the surface. For instance, viewed from outside, the cylin-
drical surface and the plane seem very different. But we will see that it would
not be easy for our two-dimensional being to decide whether it lives on a cylin-
drical surface or on a plane. If we took small pieces of plane and cylinder, it
would be impossible to make a decision by means of measurements within the
surface. By contrast, it would be relatively easy to distinguish between a piece
of a sphere and a piece of a plane.
We will now start to say more precisely which quantities can be observed
by an inhabitant of the plane.
149

150
THE INNER GEOMETRY OF SURFACES
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.1.1
Let S1 and S2 be regular surfaces in R3. A smooth map
f : S1 ‚ÜíS2 is a local isometry, if for every point p ‚ààS1 the differential
dpf : TpS1 ‚ÜíTf( p)S2
is a linear isometry regarding the Ô¨Årst fundamental form, i.e.
(
dpf(X), dpf(Y)
)
= ‚ü®X, Y‚ü©
for all X, Y ‚ààTpS1.

Everything which can be ‚Äúmeasured‚Äù within the surface, e.g. the lengths of
curves which lie on the surface or the angle between two tangent vectors,
depends on the Ô¨Årst fundamental form. Thus, if such a local isometry f exists,
then the angle between the two vectors in the image is the same as the one
between the two original tangent vectors, for example. ‚ÄúSmall‚Äù open subsets
U ‚äÇS1 cannot be distinguished from their isometric images f(U) ‚äÇS2 by
means of such measurements. Because of this we call geometric quantities
which do not change under local isometries quantities of the inner geometry.
What it exactly means when a geometric quantity does not change under
local isometries depends on the type of the mathematical object. If the geo-
metric quantity is a function FS : S ‚ÜíR in the plane, e.g. the Gauss curvature
or the mean curvature, then this means that every local isometry f : S1 ‚ÜíS2
satisÔ¨Åes
FS1 = FS2 ‚ó¶f.
Example 4.1.2
The mean curvature H is not a quantity of the inner
geometry,
since in the plane Hplane ‚â°0,
whereas in the cylindrical
surface Hcylinder ‚â°1
2.
Since plane and cylinder are locally isometric,

4.1
ISOMETRIES
151
Hcylinder = Hplane ‚ó¶f would have to apply if the mean curvature was a quantity
of the inner geometry.
Since the mean curvature is not a quantity of the inner geometry, the principal
curvatures, from which the mean curvature is calculated, cannot be quantities
of the inner geometry either. We will see later that the Gauss curvature by
contrast is a quantity of the inner geometry, although it is also calculated from
the principal curvatures.
Remark
Since the differential of a local isometry in particular always has
full rank, a local isometry is always a local diffeomorphism by the inverse map-
ping theorem. However, in general it is not a global diffeomorphism, i.e. not
bijective, as the example of the plane and cylinder has already shown.

DeÔ¨Ånition 4.1.3
A local isometry f : S1 ‚ÜíS2 which is in addition bijective,
is called an isometry. If there exists such an isometry f : S1 ‚ÜíS2, then the
surfaces S1 and S2 are said to be isometric. The surfaces S1 and S2 are called
locally isometric if for every point p ‚ààS1 there exists an open neighbourhood
U1 ‚äÇS1 of p, an open subset U2 ‚äÇS2 and an isometry f : U1 ‚ÜíU2 and
conversely for every point q ‚ààS2 an open neighbourhood U2 ‚äÇS2 of q, an
open subset U1 ‚äÇS1 and an isometry f : U2 ‚ÜíU1.
Exercise 4.4
Show that if f : S1 ‚ÜíS2 is an isometry, then f ‚àí1 : S2 ‚ÜíS1 is
also an isometry.
Exercise 4.5
Show that if there exists a surjective local isometry f : S1 ‚Üí
S2, then S1 and S2 are locally isometric. Is this also true if f is not surjective?
For example the cylinder and the plane are locally isometric. But they are
not isometric, since they are not even diffeomorphic. The relations ‚Äúisomet-
ric‚Äù and ‚Äúlocally isometric‚Äù are obviously equivalence relations on the set of
regular surfaces.

4.2

DeÔ¨Ånition 4.2.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface. A vector Ô¨Åeld on S is a map
v : S ‚ÜíR3, such that v( p) ‚ààTpS for all p ‚ààS.
A vector Ô¨Åeld assigns each point on the surface a vector, which at that point
is tangential to the surface. We also talk about continuous, differentiable,
smooth, . . . vector Ô¨Åelds, if map v has the corresponding property.
Example 4.2.2
Let f : S ‚ÜíR be a smooth function. Since the Ô¨Årst funda-
mental form is non-degenerate, there exists, for a Ô¨Åxed point p, exactly one
vector v( p) ‚ààTpS with the property
dpf(X) = I(v( p), X)
for all X ‚ààTpS. In this way the gradient vector Ô¨Åeld v =: grad f is deÔ¨Åned. We
will see soon that the gradient vector Ô¨Åeld is a smooth vector Ô¨Åeld.
The differentiability of a vector Ô¨Åeld is best veriÔ¨Åed using a local parametri-
sation. Let (U, F, V) be a local parametrisation of the regular surface S. Then
for every point p ‚ààV the vectors (‚àÇF/‚àÇu1)(F‚àí1( p)) and (‚àÇF/‚àÇu2)(F‚àí1( p))
form a basis of TpS. Hence, a vector Ô¨Åeld v over S is for all p ‚ààV uniquely
representable in the following form:
v( p) =

For instance, if one wants to study the velocity Ô¨Åeld of a curve which lies
on a plane, then the concept of vector Ô¨Åelds used up to now is not suitable
for the following two reasons. Firstly, the velocity Ô¨Åeld is not deÔ¨Åned on the
whole surface, but only along the curve. Secondly, the curve may intersect
itself, leading to the problem that the velocity vector has two different values
at this point of intersection. Considered as a vector Ô¨Åeld on the surface it would
not be uniquely determined at such points. Hence we introduce the following
deÔ¨Ånition.

4.2
VECTOR FIELDS AND THE COVARIANT DERIVATIVE
155
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.2.6
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface, and let c : I ‚ÜíS be a
parametrised curve. A vector Ô¨Åeld on S along c is a map v : I ‚ÜíR3, such
that v(t) ‚ààTc(t)S for all t ‚ààI.
Example 4.2.7
The velocity Ô¨Åeld v(t) = Àôc(t) is such a vector Ô¨Åeld along c.
Example 4.2.8
Let S be a ruled surface given by the parametrisation
F(t, s) = c(t) + sv(t) as in (3.10). Then v is a vector Ô¨Åeld on S along the curve c.
Now we need a useful concept of the derivative of such vector Ô¨Åelds. If one
naively differentiates a differentiable vector Ô¨Åeld v on S along c, then one
obtains a map Àôv : I ‚ÜíR3. The problem now is that Àôv is generally not tangential
to the surface. As often in mathematics, we solve the problem by imposing the
desired property. We replace Àôv by the projection of Àôv on the tangential plane
at the corresponding point of the curve.
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.2.9
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface,
let c : I ‚ÜíS be a
parametrised curve, and let v : I ‚ÜíR3 be a differentiable vector Ô¨Åeld on S
along c. For every point p ‚ààS, let p : R3 ‚ÜíTpS be the orthogonal projection,
i.e. if N( p) is one of the two unit normal vectors on S at the point p, then
p(X) = X ‚àí‚ü®X, N( p)‚ü©N( p).
Then
‚àá

t ‚ààI, is called the covariant derivative of v.
Hence, (‚àá/dt)v is also a vector Ô¨Åeld on S along c.
Example 4.2.10
Let S = R2 √ó {0} be the x‚Äìy plane and c a parametrised
plane curve, c(t) = (c1(t), c2(t), 0)‚ä§. A vector Ô¨Åeld v on S along c is then of the

Lemma 4.2.17
Let S be a regular surface, let c1, c2 ‚ààR, v, v1, v2, w, w1 and
w2 be differentiable vector Ô¨Åelds on S, and let f : S ‚ÜíR be a differentiable
function.
Then the following hold:
(a)
linearity in the vector Ô¨Åeld that is differentiated:
‚àáw(c1v1 + c2v2) = c1‚àáwv1 + c2‚àáwv2;
(b)

‚àáw(fv) = df(w)v + f‚àáwv;
(c)

‚àÇw(I(v1, v2)) = I(‚àáwv1, v2) + I(v1, ‚àáwv2);
(d)

‚àá(c1w1+c2w2)v = c1‚àáw1v + c2‚àáw2v;
(e)
linearity w.r.t. functions on the vector Ô¨Åeld w.r.t. which we differentiate:
‚àáfwv = f‚àáwv.
Proof
Properties (a), (b) and (c) follow directly from the corresponding
properties of the covariant derivatives of vector Ô¨Åelds along curves in lemma
4.2.12. Points (d) and (e) are most easily deduced from the formula in terms of
a local parametrisation, given in the above exercise.
4.3
Riemann curvature tensor and Theorema Egregium
In chapter 3 we met several notions of curvature, such as the principal cur-
vatures, the mean curvature and the Gauss curvature. In this section we will
introduce a new quantity, the Riemann curvature tensor. We will begin by
looking at the second covariant derivative. The value of the second covariant
derivative depends on the order in which we differentiate, i.e. the theorem of
Schwarz about the interchangeability of directional derivatives does not hold
for the covariant derivative! The Riemann curvature tensor measures exactly
this error that arises when interchanging the derivatives.
To begin with, we will examine how the second covariant derivative should
be deÔ¨Åned correctly. If v, w and z are vector Ô¨Åelds on a regular surface S, then
one can of course differentiate the vector Ô¨Åeld ‚àáwz once more with respect to v
in a covariant way. But this will introduce derivatives of w with respect to v.
Hence, if we are only interested in the second derivative of z in direction

4.3
RIEMANN CURVATURE TENSOR AND THEOREMA EGREGIUM
161
v and w, then we have to compensate for this effect and make the following
deÔ¨Ånition.

w is deÔ¨Åned via
‚àá2
v,wz := ‚àáv(‚àáwz) ‚àí‚àá‚àávwz.

A remarkable feature of Gauss‚Äôs equation is that the left hand side, i.e. the
Riemann curvature tensor, is a quantity of the inner geometry, while the quan-
tities on the right hand side of the equation, i.e. the second fundamental form
and the Weingarten map, are quantities that do not only depend on the inner
geometry of the surface. This now allows us to prove that Gauss curvature,
unlike mean curvature, is a quantity of the inner geometry of the surface.
Theorem 4.3.8 (Theorema Egregium)
Gauss curvature can be calculated form
the Riemann curvature tensor as follows: let p ‚ààS be a point. Choose an
orthonormal basis v, w of TpS. Then
K( p) = I(Rp(v, w)w, v).
In particular, Gauss curvature depends on the inner geometry of the surface
only.
Proof
According to the Gauss equation we have
I(R(v, w)w, v) = I(II(w, w) ¬∑ W(v) ‚àíII(v, w) ¬∑ W(w), v)
= II(w, w)II(v, v) ‚àíII(v, w)II(w, v)
= det(W)
= K.
Example 4.3.9
The two regular surfaces S1 = {(x, y, 0)‚ä§| x2 +y2 < 1} (circu-
lar disc) and S2 = {(x, y, z)‚ä§| x2 + y2 < 1, z =
3
1 ‚àíx2 ‚àíy2} (hemisphere) are
diffeomorphic. The projection (x, y, z)‚ä§‚Üí(x, y, 0)‚ä§gives a diffeomorphism

166
THE INNER GEOMETRY OF SURFACES
from S2 to S1. However, there cannot be an isometry between those two
surfaces, since S1 has Gauss curvature KS1 ‚â°0, while for S2 we have KS2 ‚â°1.
Since, as we now know, Gauss curvature is preserved under isometries, such an
isometry from S1 to S2 cannot exist. Our ‚Äúinhabitant of the surface‚Äù from the
beginning of this chapter can therefore use the Gauss curvature of the surface
in order to determine whether he lives on a circular disc or on a sphere.
This example is of practical signiÔ¨Åcance, since it shows that in principle it is
impossible to draw absolutely correct maps. Such a map would have to transfer
ratios of lengths of a part of the sphere to scale to a part of the plane. But
this would be (up to a scaling) an isometry between two surfaces of different
curvatures. For this reason every map will distort some lengths. The larger
the area of the earth represented by the map, the larger the distortion. See
section 4.10 for more on this.
Lemma 4.3.10
Let S be a regular surface, let p ‚ààS, let v, w, x, y ‚ààTpS. The
curvature tensor has the following symmetries:
(a)
R(v, w)x = ‚àíR(w, v)x;
(b)
I(R(v, w)x, y) = ‚àíI(R(v, w)y, x);
(c)
I(R(v, w)x, y) = I(R(x, y)v, w);
(d)
Bianchi identity:
R(v, w)x + R(x, v)w + R(w, x)v = 0.
Proof
Part (a) is trivial. Statement (c) follows from Gauss‚Äôs equation:
I(R(v, w)x, y) = I(II(w, x) ¬∑ W(v) ‚àíII(v, x) ¬∑ W(w), y)
= II(w, x) ¬∑ II(v, y) ‚àíII(v, x) ¬∑ II(w, y),
since this expression does not change when interchanging the pairs (v, w) and
(x, y). Part (b) follows directly from (a) and (c). Statement (d) can also be
derived from Gauss‚Äôs equation:
R(v, w)x + R(x, v)w + R(w, x)v
= II(w, x)W(v) ‚àíII(v, x)W(w) + II(v, w)W(x)
‚àíII(x, w)W(v) + II(x, v)W(w) ‚àíII(w, v)W(x)
= 0.

The Theorema Egregium and the last statement tell us how to calculate
Gauss curvature from the curvature tensor. Conversely, the curvature tensor
can be obtained from the Gauss curvature.
Lemma 4.3.11
Let S be a regular surface, let p ‚ààS. For all v, w, x ‚ààTpS
we have
R(v, w)x = K( p) ¬∑ (I(w, x)v ‚àíI(v, x)w).
In local coordinates,
R‚Ñì
ijk = K ¬∑

gjkŒ¥‚Ñì
i ‚àígikŒ¥‚Ñì



Proof
(a) Let V be a two-dimensional real vector space. We Ô¨Årst show that
the vector space of all multi-linear maps
R : V √ó V √ó V √ó V ‚ÜíR
that satisfy the symmetries
R(v, w, x, y) = ‚àíR(w, v, x, y)
and
R(v, w, x, y) = ‚àíR(v, w, y, x)
is one-dimensional. We choose a basis e1, e2 of V and write v = v1e1 + v2e2,
w = w1e1 + w2e2, x = x1e1 + x2e2 and y = y1e1 + y2e2. It follows that
R(v, w, x, y) = (v1w2 ‚àív2w1)R(e1, e2, x, y)

Hence R1 and R2 agree. The claim follows.
We see that Gauss curvature and curvature tensor can be calculated from each
other and hence contain the same information about the surface.

4.4
Riemannian metrics
Since we will as of now just look at quantities that depend on the inner geom-
etry only, we will at this point generalise the concept of the Ô¨Årst fundamental
form. What actually is the Ô¨Årst fundamental form? It assigns a Euclidean scalar
product to each tangent plane. That this scalar product is the restriction of the
standard scalar product on R3 is of no importance for quantities that depend
on the inner geometry only. We therefore make the following deÔ¨Ånition.
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.4.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface. A Riemannian metric g on
S assigns a Euclidean scalar product gp on the tangent plane TpS to each point
p ‚ààS, such that for every local parametrisation (U, F, V) of S the functions
gij : U ‚ÜíR,
gij(u) := gF(u)
 ‚àÇF
‚àÇui (u), ‚àÇF
‚àÇu j (u)

are smooth.

RIEMANNIAN METRICS

The Ô¨Årst fundamental form is, of course, an example of a Riemannian metric.
But there are important examples of Riemannian metrics that are not the Ô¨Årst
fundamental form, as we will see later. One of them is the hyperbolic plane.
The condition that the functions gij must be smooth ensures the scalar product
gp does not ‚Äúwildly‚Äù depend on p, but is differentiable w.r.t. p. In the case of
the Ô¨Årst fundamental form this holds automatically.
All quantities that depend on the inner geometry only, such as the surface
element, the covariant derivative, the Gauss curvature and the Riemann cur-
vature tensor, are also deÔ¨Åned for regular surfaces with a Riemannian metric
that is not the Ô¨Årst fundamental form.
We therefore deÔ¨Åne, for example, the Christoffel symbols via the formula
from lemma 4.2.14 as

A very different approach for the construction of Riemannian metrics con-

DeÔ¨Ånition 4.4.3
Let S1 and S2 be regular surfaces, let  : S1 ‚ÜíS2 be a dif-

metric ‚àóg on S1 is deÔ¨Åned by
(‚àóg)p(X, Y) := g( p)(dp(X), dp(Y))

One easily sees that ‚àóg is a Riemannian metric on S1. Indeed, ‚àóg is the
unique Riemannian metric on S1 for which  : S1 ‚ÜíS2 is an isometry. If F is
a local parametrisation of S1, then  ‚ó¶F is a local parametrisation of S2. From
the deÔ¨Ånition of ‚àóg it follows immediately that ‚àóg in the coordinates given
by F looks exactly like g with respect to  ‚ó¶F:
(‚àóg)ij = gij.
Accordingly, all quantities that only depend on the inner geometry agree, e.g.
for the Gauss curvature we have
K‚àóg = Kg ‚ó¶.

metric on S2 that does not agree with the Ô¨Årst fundamental form of S2, since
the Gauss curvature of the ellipsoid is not constant, and hence the one of the
pulled-back metric is not constant either.

4.5

We now want to investigate the following problem. In the plane the short-

a straight line. What do shortest connecting curves look like on a regular
surface?

In conclusion we can note that a connecting curve has minimal energy if and

It is for this reason that cyclists like to jump red trafÔ¨Åc lights. Although the
distance covered would not be increased by stopping, the energy needed would
be higher.

In general, the converses of the lower two arrows are not true. In the above
example with the sphere S = S2 the lines of latitude are parametrised pro-
portional to arc-length for all Œ∏, yet only the equator is a geodesic. Thus the
converse of the lower arrow is not true. Further, the equator, traversed once,
c(t) = (cos(t), sin(t), 0)‚ä§, t ‚àà[0, 2œÄ], is a geodesic. However, the length- and
energy-minimising connecting curve of p = (1, 0, 0)‚ä§with itself is, of course,
the constant curve, Àúc(t) = (1, 0, 0)‚ä§. Hence, in general, the converse of the
middle arrow is also not true.

Theorem 4.5.11 (Existence of geodesics)
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface with
Riemannian metric g. Let p ‚ààS, v ‚ààTpS and t0 ‚ààR.
Then there is an interval I ‚äÇR with t0 ‚ààI and a geodesic c : I ‚ÜíS with the
‚Äúinitial conditions‚Äù
c(t0) = p
and
Àôc(t0) = v.
Proof
We choose a local parametrisation (U, F, V) such that p ‚ààV. We set
u0 := F‚àí1( p) ‚ààU and X := (Du0F)‚àí1(v) ‚ààR2. By the existence theorem for
ordinary differential equations we can now solve (4.11) with the initial condi-
tions u(t0) = u0 and Àôu(t0) = X. With c := F ‚ó¶u we have found a geodesic with
the desired properties.
Remark
It is in some cases possible to solve the geodesic equation com-
pletely in R, i.e. we can choose I = R. In the case that S is the x‚Äìy plane or
the sphere, the geodesics (straight line and great circle, respectively) indeed
have a maximal domain I = R. If we consider, for example, the unit disc

4.5
GEODESICS
179
S = {(x, y, 0)‚ä§| x2 + y2 < 1} in the x‚Äìy plane, then the geodesic equation is the
same as for the x‚Äìy plane itself, i.e. the geodesics are the straight lines that are
traversed at constant speed, but those leave the unit disc at a certain point. The
maximal domain I of a geodesic is therefore bounded on both sides, unless the
geodesic is a constant curve.
Theorem 4.5.12 (Uniqueness of geodesics)
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a regular surface with
Riemannian metric g. Let I be an interval, t0 ‚ààI. Let c : I ‚ÜíS be a geodesic.
Then c is uniquely determined by c(t0) ‚ààS and Àôc(t0) ‚ààTc(t0)S.
Proof
If we knew that the trace of c lay entirely in one open neighbourhood
of the local parametrisation, then we could argue in a way similar to that used
for theorem 4.5.11. We would simply cite the uniqueness statement instead of
the existence statement from the theory of ordinary differential equations.
But since we cannot assume this, we need to argue a bit more carefully. Let c1
and c2 be geodesics with the same initial conditions c1(t0) = c2(t0) and Àôc1(t0) =
Àôc2(t0). Suppose that there exists a t ‚ààI, t > t0, with c1(t) Ã∏= c2(t). We set
t1 := sup{t ‚ààI | t > t0, such that c1(œÑ) = c2(œÑ) for all œÑ ‚àà[t0, t]}.
In words, t1 is exactly the point at which c1 and c2 cease to agree. We now
choose a local parametrisation (U, F, V) with c1(t1) ‚ààV. Since c1(t) = c2(t) for
all t < t1 (and t ‚â•t0) we have that
c1(t1) = c2(t1)
as well as
Àôc1(t1) = Àôc2(t1).
The uniqueness theorem for ordinary differential equations now tells us that
c1(t) = c2(t) as long as c1(t) ‚ààV and c2(t) ‚ààV. For a sufÔ¨Åciently small Œµ > 0
this is the case for all t ‚àà(t1 ‚àíŒµ, t1 + Œµ). This contradicts the maximality of t1.
It follows that c1(t) = c2(t) for all t ‚â•t0. The proof for t ‚â§t0 is analogous.

Exercise 4.22
(a) Show that on surfaces of revolution the lines of longitude
t ‚ÜíF(t, œï0) are length-minimising connecting curves of their end-points (and
hence in particular geodesics after a reparametrisation by arc-length).
(b) What is the condition for a line of latitude œï ‚ÜíF(t0, œï) to be a geodesic?
Let us now return from surfaces of revolution to general regular surfaces
with Riemannian metrics. We have seen that geodesics generalise the straight
lines from plane geometry. Let us recall that the curvature of plane curves is
deÔ¨Åned as the deviation from a straight line. We can generalise this deÔ¨Ånition
to curves in general (oriented) surfaces. We deÔ¨Åne them as the deviation from
a geodesic.
Let S be an oriented regular surface with Riemannian metric g. Let c :
I ‚ÜíS be a curve parametrised by arc-length. Let n : I ‚ÜíR3 be the unit
normal Ô¨Åeld along c that complements Àôc to positively oriented orthonormal
bases, i.e. for every t ‚ààI the pair (Àôc(t), n(t)) is a positively oriented orthonor-
mal basis of Tc(t)S. Just like with plane curves differentiating the function
t ‚Üígc(t)(Àôc(t), Àôc(t)) shows that (‚àá/dt)Àôc(t) is perpendicular to Àôc(t). There is
therefore a unique function Œ∫g : I ‚ÜíR such that
‚àá
dt Àôc(t) = Œ∫g(t) ¬∑ n(t).
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.5.14
The function Œ∫g is called the geodesic curvature of c in S
w.r.t. g.
It is clear from the deÔ¨Ånition that c is a geodesic if and only if Œ∫g ‚â°0. The
geodesic curvature generalises the curvature of plane curves. If S is the x‚Äì
y plane with the Ô¨Årst fundamental form as a Riemannian metric, then Œ∫g is
exactly the curvature of c, considered as a plane curve.
If we change the direction in which the curve is traversed, i.e. if we change
c to Àúc(t) := c(‚àít), then Œ∫g changes its sign, ÀúŒ∫g(t) = ‚àíŒ∫g(‚àít). Leaving curve c
as it is and reversing the orientation of the surface S we change n to ‚àín, and
Œ∫g changes its sign, ÀúŒ∫g(t) = ‚àíŒ∫g(t). It follows that for non-oriented surfaces at
least the absolute value of the geodesic curvature is |Œ∫g| is still well deÔ¨Åned.
