4.6
The exponential map
We now want to use geodesics to construct local parametrisations of surfaces
that are particularly well suited to the geometry. Among other things this is
important to make calculations easier to carry out; we have already seen in
examples that the complexity of formulae can depend heavily on the coordi-
nates used. As surfaces on the small scale are approximated by their tangent
plane, we will try to translate the coordinates most commonly used on planes,
i.e. Cartesian and polar coordinates, from the tangent plane to the surface
itself. For this purpose we construct, using geodesics, geometrically natural
maps from the tangent planes to the surface.
Let S be a regular surface with Riemannian metric g. Let p ‚ààS be a point.
For a tangent vector v ‚ààTpS we consider the (unique) geodesic c : I ‚ÜíS with
c(0) = p, Àôc(0) = v and maximal domain interval I. If c is still deÔ¨Åned at time
t = 1, i.e. if 1 ‚ààI, then we set
expp(v) := c(1).
If expp is deÔ¨Åned for v ‚ààTpS and if Œ¥ ‚àà[0, 1], then expp is also deÔ¨Åned for
Œ¥v ‚ààTpS. If cv is the geodesic with cv(0) = p and Àôc(0) = v, cŒ¥v is analogously
the geodesic with cŒ¥v(0) = p and Àôc(0) = Œ¥v, then cŒ¥v(t) = cv(Œ¥t), see exercise
4.21. Hence cŒ¥v is deÔ¨Åned at t = 1, since cv(0) = p is determined on the whole
of [0, 1]. This argument shows that the domain Dp ‚äÇTpS of expp is a star-
shaped subset of TpS with respect to 0. The argument also shows that
cv(t) = expp(tv).
(4.12)
The domain is certainly not empty, since the constant geodesic c(t) ‚â°p is
deÔ¨Åned on the whole of R, in particular for t = 1, and hence 0 ‚ààDp. The
theorem about the dependence of solutions of ordinary differential equations
on the initial values further ensures that Dp is an open subset of TpS and that
expp : Dp ‚ÜíS
is a smooth map.
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.6.1
The map expp : Dp ‚ÜíS is called the exponential map.

Example 4.6.2
Let S = R2 √ó {0} be the x‚Äìy plane with the Ô¨Årst fundamental
form as Riemannian metric. Let p ‚ààS and v ‚ààTpS = R2 √ó {0}. The geodesic
c in S with c(0) = p and Àôc(0) = v is the straight line c(t) = p + tv. Hence
Dp = TpS = R2 √ó {0} and
expp(v) = p + v.
Example 4.6.3
Now let S = {(x, y, 0)‚ä§‚ààR3 | x2 + y2 < 1} be the unit disc
in the x‚Äìy plane with the Ô¨Årst fundamental form as a Riemannian metric, as
above. The geodesics are again segments of straight lines, but as they leave the
disc after a Ô¨Ånite amount of time, the domain of the (non-constant) geodesic
is always a Ô¨Ånite interval. For the exponential map we have the same formula
as above:
expp(v) = p + v,
but the domain of the exponential map is now
Dp = {v ‚ààR3 | p + v ‚ààS} = S ‚àíp.
Example 4.6.4

We want to use the exponential map to translate coordinates from the tan-
gent plane to the surface. However, the example of the sphere shows that the
exponential map need not be bijective. We therefore have to restrict it. The
inverse function theorem will then tell us that the exponential map, restricted
to a neighbourhood of 0, is a diffeomorphism. To be able to use the inverse
function theorem, we calculate the differential of the exponential map at the
point 0 ‚ààDp. We observe that T0Dp = T0TpS = TpS.

Corollary 4.6.11
If S1 and S2 are two regular surfaces with the same constant
Gauss curvature Œ∫, then S1 and S2 are locally isometric.
We now see that the inhabitants of the surface from the introduction of this
chapter about the inner geometry of surfaces cannot distinguish different
surfaces with the same constant Gauss curvature if they can only take mea-
surements in the close neighbourhood of a point. In particular, they cannot
distinguish the plane, the cylinder and the cone.

4.7
Parallel transport
If we have two points p and q in a plane E ‚äÇR3, then we can identify tangent
vectors from TpE with those from TqE, since TpE = TqE = E. If we now
replace E by a general regular surface S ‚äÇR3, then this is no longer straight-
forward, since in general TpS Ã∏= TqS. In order still to be able to relate tangent
planes at different points even on general surfaces we analyse the concept of
‚Äúparallel‚Äù vectors in the plane a bit more carefully.
We can translate a vector v0 ‚ààTpE into the corresponding (the same)
vector from TqE in the following way: we choose a connecting smooth curve
c : [a, b] ‚ÜíE, c(a) = p, c(b) = q, and consider along c the constant vector

Ô¨Åeld v(t) ‚â°v0 or, put differently, the vector Ô¨Åeld along c that satisÔ¨Åes Àôv ‚â°0.
Then v(b) ‚ààTqE is the vector translated to q. This approach can be used with-
out complications on general regular surfaces by simply replacing the usual
derivative of v by the covariant derivative.

called parallel transport along c.

4.8
Jacobi Ô¨Åelds
We discussed the geometric meaning of Gauss curvature in section 3.6. Theo-
rem 3.6.15 told us how the surface behaves near a point relative to the tangent
plane at that point, in terms of the of the sign of the Gauss curvature at that
point. But those investigations are only valid for the Ô¨Årst fundamental form. In
the case of a general Riemannian metric we want to understand what Gauss
curvature tells us about the inner geometry of a surface. Jacobi Ô¨Åelds are an
important tool for this; they, as we will see below, contain information about
the behaviour of neighbouring geodesics.
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.8.1
Let S be a regular surface with Riemannian metric g. Let
c be a geodesic on S. A smooth vector Ô¨Åeld J on S along c is called a Jacobi
Ô¨Åeld if

the set of all Jacobi Ô¨Åelds forms a four-dimensional vector space.
To what extent do Jacobi Ô¨Åelds tell us something about the behaviour of
neighbouring geodesics?

we know from straight lines in the Euclidean plane.

the geodesics diverge.

4.9
Spherical and hyperbolic geometry
We now want to investigate spherical geometry in more detail, in particular
with regard to trigonometric rules analogous to those of Euclidean geome-
try, which we treated in the Ô¨Årst chapter. At the same time we introduce a
surface which is in a way the negatively curved analogue of the sphere, the
hyperbolic plane. We can investigate its trigonometry simultaneously. The
hyperbolic plane has great historic importance, since it satisÔ¨Åes all axioms of
Euclidean geometry except for the parallel axiom. It also shows that the efforts
to derive the parallel axiom from the other axioms, which had been going on
for thousands of years, were inevitably in vain.

4.10
Cartography
Cartography is the science of geographical maps. Mathematically speaking,
we want to study charts of S2. For simplicity, we will ignore all complications
arising from the fact that the earth is better described by a certain rotational
ellipsoid than by a sphere. Ideally, one would like to Ô¨Ånd charts which preserve
lengths, angles, and areas up to a scale factor. However, this is not possible
since such a chart would be an isometry between a portion of S2 and a portion
of R2. By Gauss‚Äôs Theorema Egregium no such isometry exists because S2 has
Gauss curvature K ‚â°1 while the Euclidean plane has K ‚â°0. In the literature
on cartography one nevertheless Ô¨Ånds statements of the extent that certain
maps are length-preserving. This is rather misleading because it means that
some lengths are preserved, not all.
As we will see it is possible to construct charts that are angle-preserving or
area-preserving. A chart is area-preserving if the area element of the sphere
expressed in the coordinates u1, u2 coincides with the area element of the
Euclidean metric of the plane, dA = du1 du2. How can we see whether or
not a chart is angle-preserving?
Lemma 4.10.1
Let g and g‚Ä≤ be two Euclidean metrics on Rn, n ‚â•2. Then g
and g‚Ä≤ deÔ¨Åne the same angles if and only if there exists a number c > 0 such that
g‚Ä≤ = c ¬∑ g.
Proof
If g‚Ä≤ = c ¬∑ g, then for any non-zero vectors X, Y ‚ààRn we have
g(X, Y)

g(X, X)
3
g(Y, Y)
=
g‚Ä≤(X, Y)
3
g‚Ä≤(X, X)
3
g‚Ä≤(Y, Y)
,
because the factor c cancels. Thus the angles between X and Y are the same
for g and for g‚Ä≤.
Conversely, let g and g‚Ä≤ deÔ¨Åne the same angles. Fix a non-zero vector Y ‚ààRn.
For any X ‚ààRn, not a multiple of Y, consider the triangle with vertices 0, Y
and X. Denote the angle at the corner X by Œ∑ and the one at the corner Y by
Œæ. It is important that the angles are the same for g and for g‚Ä≤. By the sine rule
we have

Hence this chart is also neither area- nor angle-preserving but it has the
pleasing property that the geodesics on the sphere, the great circles, are
represented in the chart by straight lines. This is because a great circle is the
intersection of S2 with a plane E through the origin which is projected to the
intersection of E with the tangent plane, a straight line. Thus this type of map
is well suited for Ô¨Ånding the shortest route from one point to another.

pole we obtain the stereographic projection.

map representation of a neighbourhood of a circle of latitude other than the
equator, one can wrap a cone around the sphere along a small circle and then
unwrap it. The interested reader should consult a book on cartography such
as [28].

Exercise 4.36
Show that the hyperbolic plane has inÔ¨Ånite area.
The metric does not look particularly simple in the Klein model but this
model has one very pleasing feature: geodesics in M‚àí1 correspond to straight
line segments in U. This is clear from the construction of F. The geodesics
in M‚àí1 are the intersections of M‚àí1 with planes E containing the origin.
Under the central projection this is mapped to the intersection of E with the
unit disc in Te1M‚àí1. The intersection of the two planes E and Te1M‚àí1 is a
straight line.
This allows a simple discussion of the parallel axiom. Keeping the terminol-
ogy and the notation from the Ô¨Årst chapter we now take the hyperbolic plane
M‚àí1 as the set of points, the set of ‚Äústraight lines‚Äù will be the set of traces of
all geodesics,
P := M‚àí1,
G := {M‚àí1 ‚à©E| E ‚äÇR3 is a two-dimensional subspace} ‚àí{‚àÖ}.
The incidence relation is the usual set-theoretical inclusion. We immediately
see that the incidence axioms I1‚ÄìI4 are satisÔ¨Åed. Unlike in spherical geome-
try, in which geodesics are great circles, there is an obvious useful deÔ¨Ånition
for when one point lies between two others on the hyperbolas in hyperbolic
geometry. The validity of the ordering axioms A1‚ÄìA5 is then easy to see. For
the deÔ¨Ånition of congruence of lengths and angles we use isometries as in the
discussion of the Cartesian model of Euclidean geometry. This time we use
the group G‚àí1 of isometries on M‚àí1 instead of the Euclidean group. It is then
not difÔ¨Åcult to show the validity of the congruence axioms. By theorem 1.1.9
we already know that parallels therefore exist. The completeness axioms can
be derived in a way similar to the one used for the Cartesian model.
Only the parallel axiom is not valid, since parallels are not unique. This is
easily seen in the Klein model. Given a line segment L in U there is an inÔ¨Ånity
of line segments not intersecting L but having one point in common.

5 Geometry and analysis
Surfaces with boundary are introduced. The divergence theorem of Gauss is
derived and used to show that the total Gauss curvature of a compact regular
surface does not depend on the Riemannian metric.
5.1
The divergence theorem
In this section we want to derive a two-dimensional analogue of the funda-
mental theorem of calculus. In this theorem the integral of a derivative over a
one-dimensional interval is identiÔ¨Åed with the difference of the values at the
end-points. This term in the values at the end-points can be considered as the
integral of the function over the (zero-dimensional) boundary of the interval.
The divergence theorem expresses the integral of a derivative of a vector Ô¨Åeld
as a one-dimensional line integral. To make all this precise we Ô¨Årst need the
notion of a surface with boundary.
DeÔ¨Ånition 5.1.1
A surface with boundary is a closed subset S of a reg-
ular surface Sreg ‚äÇR3 such that for every point p ‚ààS there exists a local
parametrisation F : U ‚ÜíSreg of Sreg with p ‚ààF(U), such that either
‚Ä¢
F(U) ‚äÇS (then p is called an interior point of S) or
‚Ä¢
F‚àí1( p) = (x, 0)‚ä§for an x ‚ààR and F‚àí1(S) = {(x, y)‚ä§‚ààU| y ‚â•0} (then p is
called a boundary point of S).
The set of all interior points is the interior of S, the set of all boundary points
is the boundary ‚àÇS.
Example 5.1.2
The closed circular disc S = {(x, y, 0)‚ä§| x2 + y2 ‚â§1} is a
surface with boundary. We can take the x‚Äìy plane as the regular surface Sreg =
R2 √ó {0}. The points (x, y, 0)‚ä§with x2 + y2 < 1 are interior points, since for

We have seen in example 4.2.2 that differentiation of a function f on a regu-
lar surface yields a vector Ô¨Åeld grad f, the gradient of f, on the surface. The
statements from examples 4.2.2 and 4.2.3 remain valid if we endow the regular
surface with an arbitrary Riemannian metric instead of the Ô¨Årst fundamental
form. However, for a given f the gradient also depends on the choice of the
Riemannian metric; this is not the case for the differential dpf.

The boundary term in the divergence theorem measures the proportion of the
vector Ô¨Åeld that points out of the surface with boundary S. If we imagine the
vector Ô¨Åeld X as the current density of a liquid that Ô¨Çows on the regular surface
Sreg, then the boundary term gives the net amount of liquid that Ô¨Çows out of
S. In particular, if the Ô¨Åeld is divergence-free, i.e. divX ‚â°0, then the amount
of liquid in S remains unchanged. Arguments of this type are important in the
derivations of many conservation laws in physics.
The divergence theorem now also gives us a graphic interpretation of the
divergence of a vector Ô¨Åeld. If p is a point on the regular surface, then we take
the circular disc ¬ØD( p, r) with centre p and radius r as the regular surface with
boundary, see lemma 4.6.12. The mean of the divergence on this disc is by the
divergence theorem


A[ ¬ØD( p, r)]
=

‚àÇ¬ØD( p,r) g(X, ŒΩ)ds

divX( p) = lim


‚àÇ¬ØD( p,r) g(X, ŒΩ)ds
A[ ¬ØD( p, r)]
.
The divergence at p therefore tells us how far the vector Ô¨Åeld at the point p
points out of the disc. If the divergence at p is positive, then we can imagine p
as a source for the vector Ô¨Åeld, if it is negative then p is a sink.

such tensor Ô¨Åelds. However, as we do not need this, we will omit the details.
5.2
Variation of the metric
We will spend the rest of the chapter discussing how geometric quantities,
e.g. the surface element or the Gauss curvature, change if we distort the
Riemannian metric.

Theorem 5.2.7
Let S be a compact regular surface. Then the number

Example 5.2.9

If one wants to show that two given surfaces, e.g. the sphere and the ellipsoid,
are diffeomorphic, then one will try to construct a diffeomorphism. But how
can one show that two surfaces are not diffeomorphic? The number

S K dA
gives us the possibility to distinguish surfaces that are not diffeomorphic, e.g.
the sphere and the torus. In the next chapter we will learn to understand this
number better by showing how we can Ô¨Ånd it by cutting the surface into trian-
gles and then counting vertices, edges and triangles. This will be the subject of
the Gauss‚ÄìBonnet theorem.

6 Geometry and topology
Differential geometry is a Ô¨Åne, quantitative geometry, in which relationships
between lengths and angles are important. Topology, by contrast, is of a much
coarser and more qualitative nature. Here only those quantities that are pre-
served under distortions are studied. In order to obtain a topological description
of the total Gauss curvature, we triangulate the surfaces, i.e. we cut them into tri-
angles. The theorem of Gauss‚ÄìBonnet now tells us that we can determine the
total curvature by counting vertices, edges and triangles.
6.1
Polyhedra
In the last sections of this book we want to study global properties of sur-
faces. For example, we want be able to decide whether two given surfaces are
homeomorphic or not. For this purpose we will cut the surfaces into triangles,
which will then allow us to use combinatorial methods. We make the following
deÔ¨Ånition.
DeÔ¨Ånition 6.1.1
A polyhedron X is a Ô¨Ånite set of triangles j ‚äÇRn,
X = {1, . . . , k},
with the following property: any two of those triangles intersect
‚Ä¢
not at all, or
‚Ä¢
exactly at one vertex, or
‚Ä¢
exactly at one edge.
The union of those triangles,
|X| := ‚à™k
j=1j,
is called the geometric realisation of X.

not polyhedra
We use the following notation:
e(X) = number of vertices of X,
k(X) = number of edges of X,
f(X) = number of triangles X.
DeÔ¨Ånition 6.1.2
The number
œá(X) := e(X) ‚àík(X) + f(X)
is called the Euler‚ÄìPoincar√© characteristic of X.

Let us take a tetrahedron in R3 as X:

Remark

realisation of a polyhedron, then e(X), k(X) and f(X) are unsuitable, but the
Euler‚ÄìPoincar√© characteristic œá(X) is a good candidate.

6.2
Triangulations
We want to decompose surfaces into triangles to then investigate them with
combinatorial tools. Such a decomposition is called a triangulation.

6.2
TRIANGULATIONS

DeÔ¨Ånition 6.2.1
Let S ‚äÇR3 be a compact regular surface. A triangulation
of S is a pair (X, ) consisting of a polyhedron X and a homeomorphism
 : |X| ‚ÜíS,
such that for every triangle  of X the restriction | :  ‚Üí() is a
diffeomorphism. More precisely,
for every triangle  of X, there exists an open neighbourhood U of  in the
plane spanned by , and there is an open neighbourhood V of () in S and
a diffeomorphism œï : U ‚ÜíV such that œï| = |.
The diffeomorphic images () of the triangles of X decompose the surface
S into ‚Äúcurved triangles‚Äù.
Remark
A polyhedron X that belongs to a triangulation of a compact reg-
ular surface always has the property required in theorem 6.1.5, which states
that exactly two triangles meet at every edge. This is the case because a regular
surface is locally divided into exactly two parts by a regular curve.
DeÔ¨Ånition 6.2.2
Let S be a compact regular surface and let (X, ) be a
triangulation of S. Then
œá(S) := œá(X)
is called the Euler‚ÄìPoincar√© characteristic of S.

DeÔ¨Ånition 6.2.2 still needs some justiÔ¨Åcation. It is a priori possible that dif-
ferent triangulations of the same surface lead to different Euler‚ÄìPoincar√©

us that this is not the case, see corollary 6.3.3.
It also needs to be shown that a compact regular surface can always be tri-
angulated, i.e. has a triangulation. We will prove this using a series of lemmas.
The reader willing to believe in the existence of triangulations may continue
reading with theorem 6.2.17 on page 257.

Example6.2.4
In the case of the sphere S=S2 the value œÅ =1 is the maximal
œÅ that satisÔ¨Åes the condition in lemma 6.2.3, since the origin has the same

To obtain a triangulation of a regular surface S, we will construct the corre-
sponding polyhedron in such a way that all vertices lie on the surface. The
triangles, and hence the entire geometric realisation |X| of the polyhedron,
will still be contained in UœÅ(S). In particular, P : |X| ‚ÜíS is a continuous map,
restricted to each triangle even a smooth one. Nevertheless, we must proceed
very carefully positioning the triangles, since otherwise even the restriction of
P to a single triangle might not be injective. Very narrow triangles might be
aligned perpendicularly to the surface.
This effect can occur with arbitrarily small triangles, although one might
naively expect that a sufÔ¨Åciently Ô¨Åne partition into triangles will provide a
triangulation. We must therefore also ensure that the triangles are not too
narrow.

Therefore (|X|) is also an open subset of S. Assuming without loss of gen-
erality that S is connected (otherwise apply the argument to each connected
component seperately) we conclude (|X|) = S.
We summarise:
Theorem 6.2.17
Let S be a compact orientable regular surface. Then there
exists a œÅ0 > 0 such that for every Œµ-conÔ¨Åguration A with Œµ ‚àà(0, œÅ0] there is a
polyhedron X whose vertices are points in A, such that
 := P||X| : |X| ‚ÜíS
deÔ¨Ånes a triangulation. In particular, S has triangulations.
Remark
The assumption that the surface is orientable is, in fact, unnec-
essary. Compact regular surfaces in R3 are actually automatically orientable.
This is the case because such surfaces divide R3 into two parts, the inside and
the outside of the surface. At every point of the surface one of the two unit
normal vectors points inside and the other points outside. The outer and the
inner unit normal Ô¨Åeld are both smooth, and the surface is thus orientable.
Can we triangulate surfaces that are not compact? As our deÔ¨Ånitions imply
that the geometric realisation of a polyhedron is always a Ô¨Ånite union of com-
pact triangles, it is itself compact. It can therefore only be homeomorphic to a
compact surface. However, one could instead of Ô¨Ånitely many triangles allow
countably many. Then a more careful discussion than the one from the proof of
theorem 6.2.17 will show that non-compact surfaces can also be triangulated.
The details are left to the (ambitious) reader.
As a corollary of the fact that compact regular surfaces have triangulations,
we can now come back to a remark about integration theory on surfaces, which
we made in section 3.7.
Corollary 6.2.18
Let S be a compact orientable regular surface. Then there
exist three local parametrisations (Ui, Fi, Vi) of S, which cover S entirely:

6.4
Outlook
Having reached the end of this book, we have only got to know the elements
of differential geometry. The reader may want to consult other textbooks on
curves and surfaces such as [6, 9, 14, 23]. Differential geometry has devel-
oped enormously and has countless interconnections to other areas, not only
in mathematics. We want to conclude this book with some notes about such
developments and applications.
Knot theory
We have seen in section 2.3 that knots must curve quite a lot.
Knot theory, which mainly deals with the question of when two curves are
ambient isotopic, has now developed into its own branch of topology, with
some amazing connections to physics and other areas. Introductions can be
found in, for example, [15, 20, 27].
Immersions
The reader may have noticed that regular surfaces and regular
curves were deÔ¨Åned in very different ways. Curves are equivalence classes of
parametrised curves, i.e. of certain maps. Regular surfaces on the other hand
are subsets of R3 with certain properties. A notion of surfaces that is closer to
our concept of curves is the one of an immersed surface. Such an immersion is
a map F : S ‚ÜíR3, where S is a regular surface and dpF has full rank for all
p ‚ààS. Two immersions F1 and F2 are equivalent if there exists a diffeomor-
phism  : S ‚ÜíS with F2 = F1 ‚ó¶. An immersed surface is then an equiva-
lence class of immersions. As we do not require immersions to be injective,
immersed surfaces can have self-intersections, just like curves.
We have already met some immersed surfaces, e.g. Enneper‚Äôs surface from
example 3.8.12, which we then turned into regular surfaces by restricting the
domain of the immersion. A large part of the surface theory discussed can
easily be extended to immersed surfaces.
We can construct interesting non-orientable immersed surfaces that cannot
be realised in R3 without self-intersections. Examples for this are the projec-
tive plane (or crosscap) and the Klein bottle [12, 1.89]. In the Ô¨Årst example the
domain of the immersion is a sphere, in the second one a torus.
ClassiÔ¨Åcation of compact surfaces
We have seen that the sphere and the
ellipsoid are diffeomorphic, while the sphere and the torus are not. We say
a bit loosely that the sphere and the ellipsoid are topologically equivalent, the
sphere and the torus, on the other hand, are topologically different. But how
many topologically different compact surfaces are there?

6.4
OUTLOOK
263
The answer to this question is remarkably simple. The Euler‚ÄìPoincar√© charac-
teristic tells us whether two given compact surfaces are topologically equiva-
lent or not. More precisely, let S1 and S2 be two connected compact orientable
surfaces. Then the following are equivalent:
‚Ä¢
S1 and S2 are diffeomorphic.
‚Ä¢
S1 and S2 are homeomorphic.
‚Ä¢
œá(S1) = œá(S2).
Possible values for the Euler‚ÄìPoincar√© characteristic are the numbers
2, 0, ‚àí2, ‚àí4, . . . [6].
œá = 2
œá = 0
œá = ‚àí2
There is a similar classiÔ¨Åcation for non-orientable compact surfaces. We have
seen that the sphere and the torus have Riemannian metrics with constant
Gauss curvature. This is also the case for the other compact surfaces. By the
Gauss‚ÄìBonnet theorem a negative Euler‚ÄìPoincar√© characteristic implies that
the Gauss curvature is also negative. Theorem 3.6.17 tells us that such a metric
can never be the Ô¨Årst fundamental form.
Minimal surfaces
We have spent a considerable amount of time studying
minimal surfaces, but an investigation of minimal surfaces would, done prop-
erly, require an entire course. In particular, conformal parametrisations could
not be considered due to lack of space. See [1, 19, 26] for more.
Abstract manifolds
Having passed from the Ô¨Årst fundamental form to gen-
eral Riemannian metrics, we have in a way taken the regular surfaces out of
R3. They are still subsets of R3, but the relative position of the surface in space
is no longer reÔ¨Çected by the Riemannian metric in any way. It is therefore
in this context more natural to deÔ¨Åne surfaces with Riemannian metrics in a
more abstract way, not as subsets of Euclidean space, but as abstract topo-
logical spaces with certain properties and additional structures. We have here
refrained from taking this approach in order to keep the formal effort as small
as possible, and because certain constructions, such as the tangent plane, are
less intuitive.
Those concepts are nevertheless indispensable for a more detailed study
of differential geometry [5, 16, 35]. The object of study is not only the

264
GEOMETRY AND TOPOLOGY
two-dimensional case, but also the higher-dimensional ‚Äúmanifolds‚Äù, some-
times even inÔ¨Ånite-dimensional ones. A beautiful book on three-dimensional
geometry is [32].
Riemannian geometry
Further developments in the theory of the inner
geometry of abstract manifolds lead to Riemannian geometry. A part of our
discussion translates without any changes, e.g. the theory of geodesics. But
there are very new phenomena as well. Recall that for surfaces it was possible
to calculate the Gauss curvature from the Riemann curvature tensor and vice
versa. This is no longer possible in higher dimensions for the corresponding
generalisations. The curvature tensor carries more information than the scalar
curvature (one of the generalisations of the Gauss curvature). There is addi-
tionally the concept of the Ricci curvature, which lies between the two others.
See, for example, [10, 12, 29] for an introduction.
Lorentzian geometry and general relativity
Einstein used Riemannian geome-
try in his gravitation theory, the theory of general relativity. But a modiÔ¨Åcation
needs to be made here. Instead of positive-deÔ¨Ånite scalar products we consider
non-degenerate bilinear forms of index 1 on the tangent spaces. This is because
we are not modelling curved space, but curved space-time. This variant of
Riemannian geometry is called Lorentzian geometry [3, 25]. Einstein‚Äôs Ô¨Åeld
equation relates the geometric quantities, in particular the above-mentioned
Ricci curvature, to physical quantities. Very good textbooks are [31, 34], see
also [22] for an extensive introduction.
Ricci Ô¨Çow
Fix a compact regular surface S and a Riemannian metric g0 on
S. Now look at the equation
‚àÇ
‚àÇtg(t) = 2(k ‚àíK(t))g(t),
(6.5)
subject to the initial condition
g(0) = g0.
Here g(t) denotes a family of Riemannian metrics on S depending on a param-
eter t ‚àà[0, ‚àû), K(t) is the Gauss curvature with respect to g(t) and k is its mean,
k =

S K(t) dA(t)/area(S, g(t)). Equation (6.5) is known as the normalized
Ricci Ô¨Çow equation.
It turns out that for any g0 there is a unique solution and it is deÔ¨Åned for all
t ‚àà[0, ‚àû). Observe that if g0 has constant Gauss curvature, then the right hand
side of (6.5) vanishes, so the solution is constant in t, g(t) ‚â°g0. In general, it
turns out that there is a limit metric g‚àû= limt‚Üí‚àûg(t) and g‚àûhas constant
Gauss curvature. Moreover, g‚àûis conformally equivalent to g0, i.e. there is a
positive function f : S ‚ÜíR such that g‚àû= f ¬∑ g0. See [8] together with [7].

6.4
OUTLOOK
265
This shows that for any Riemannian metric g0 on S there is a conformally
equivalent metric with constant Gauss curvature. This is the geometric version
of a classical theorem, the uniformatisation theorem.
A lot of research has been done and is still being done on Ricci Ô¨Çow on
higher-dimensional manifolds. The term ‚ÄúRicci Ô¨Çow‚Äù comes from the fact that
a formulation of (6.5) in higher dimensions requires the notion of Ricci cur-
vature mentioned above. By far the most spectacular result was obtained in
dimension 3. After many failed attempts by others, Grisha Perelman was able
to prove the Poincar√© conjecture which had been open for more than a hun-
dred years. It says that any compact connected three-dimensional manifold in
which every closed curve can be shrunk to a point must be homeomorphic to
the three-dimensional sphere S3. The corresponding statement in dimension
2 is an easy consequence of the classiÔ¨Åcation of compact surfaces mentioned
earlier. In fact, Perelman even proved a stronger conjecture due to William
Thurston. See, for example, [24] for an introduction to these deep results.

(because Œª > 0).
Axiom K2: This follows from the fact that E(2) is a group.
Axiom K3: One shows that two line segments pq and p‚Ä≤q‚Ä≤ in the Cartesian
plane are congruent if and only if they have equal lengths, i.e. ‚à•p ‚àíq‚à•=
‚à•p‚Ä≤ ‚àíq‚Ä≤‚à•. Then axiom K3 is clear.
Axiom K4: This follows from the fact that E(2) is a group.
Axiom K5: There is a unique A ‚ààO(2) such that A( p ‚àíq) = Œª ¬∑ ( p1 ‚àíq1),
Œª > 0, and the determinants of the 2 √ó 2 matrices (A( p ‚àíq), A(r ‚àíq)) and
( p1 ‚àíq1, s1 ‚àíq1) have equal sign. Put b := q1 ‚àíAq and F(x) := Ax + b. Then
F ‚ààE(2) and r1 := F(r) does the job.
Axiom K6: Since ‚à†(q, p, r) ‚â°‚à†(q1, p1, r1), there exists F ‚ààE(2) such that
F( p) = p1, F(q) ‚àíF( p) = Œª ¬∑ (q1 ‚àíp1) and F(r) ‚àíF( p) = Œº ¬∑ (r1 ‚àíp1),
Œª, Œº > 0. From pq = p1q1 we conclude Œª = 1 and similarly from pr = p1r1
we derive Œº = 1. Thus F( p) = p1, F(q) = q1, and F(r) = r1. This implies all
congruences.

This local parametrisation covers all of S2 except one single point, namely

3.2
Any local parametrisation (U, F, V) of S gives rise to a local parametrisa-
tion (F‚àí1(W), F|F‚àí1(W), V ‚à©W) of S ‚à©W. These local parametrisations cover
S ‚à©W.
3.3
For any point of p ‚ààS there is an open neighbourhood V of p such that
S ‚à©V is a regular surface. Hence there is a local parametrisation (U, F, V‚Ä≤) of
S ‚à©V with p ‚ààV‚Ä≤. Now (U, F, V‚Ä≤) is also a local parametrisation of S, thus S
can be covered by local parametrisations.

3.12
(a) Let N ‚ààS2. Let E be the orthogonal complement of N. Since S is
compact the afÔ¨Åne plane Et := E + t ¬∑ N will not intersect S provided t is large
enough. Now decrease t until the afÔ¨Åne plane Et touches S for the Ô¨Årst time.
Then E is the tangent plane of S at this intersection point p, hence N is the
exterior unit normal vector to S at p.
(b) Suppose there is a point p ‚ààS such that K( p) < 0. By exercise 3.11(b)
there are points of S on both sides of the afÔ¨Åne tangent plane TpS + p. The
construction in (a) above yields a point q ‚ààS such that N(q) = N( p). By
construction S lies entirely on one side of the afÔ¨Åne tangent plane TqS + q.
Therefore q Ã∏= p and the Gauss map is not injective.

4.4
This follows from dq( f ‚àí1) = (dpf)‚àí1, where f( p) = q and the fact that

4.5
Given a local isometry f : S1 ‚ÜíS2 we can restrict it to an isometry of a
neighbourhood U of any given point p ‚ààS1 onto its image V = f(U). By
exercise 4.4 ( f|U)‚àí1 : V ‚ÜíU is also an isometry. Since f is onto we can Ô¨Ånd
such a neighbourhood V for any given point q ‚ààS2.
This last argument fails if f is not assumed surjective. For example, let S2 be
a surface having elliptic points and hyperbolic points (such as the torus). Let
S1 ‚äÇS2 be the open subset of elliptic points. Then the embedding f : S1  ‚ÜíS2
is a local isometry. But no hyperbolic point in S2 can have a neighbourhood
which is isometric to an open subset of S1 because isometries preserve Gauss
curvature as we shall see (see Theorem 4.3.8).

4.20
We know from example 4.5.9 that the equator is a geodesic when

metric image of the equator and hence, by exercise 4.18, also a geodesic when
parametrised proportional to arc-length. Simply choose A ‚ààO(3) such that it
maps the x‚Äìy plane to the plane E whose intersection with S2 is the given great
circle.

4.31
Let f : MŒ∫ ‚ÜíMŒ∫ be an isometry and let c(t) = expp(tX) be the geodesic
in MŒ∫ with initial values c(0) = p and Àôc(0) = X. Since isometries map geodesics
to geodesics, f ‚ó¶c is the geodesic with initial values f( p) and dpf(X).
Hence f(c(t)) = expf( p)(tdpf(X)). In particular, for t = 1 we get f(expp(X)) =
expf( p)(dpf(X)). On MŒ∫ any two points p and q can be joined by a geodesic;
just intersect MŒ∫ with the plane containing p, q and 0. Thus expp : TpMŒ∫ ‚ÜíMŒ∫
is surjective. Hence for any q ‚ààMŒ∫ we can Ô¨Ånd an X ‚ààTpMŒ∫ with expp(X) = q.
Then f(q) = expf( p)(dpf(X)). This shows that f is uniquely detemined by the
point f( p) and the linear map dpf : TpMŒ∫ ‚ÜíTf( p)MŒ∫.
Now Ô¨Åx p = (0, 0, 1)‚ä§‚ààMŒ∫. For any q ‚ààMŒ∫ we can Ô¨Ånd an f ‚ààGŒ∫ with f( p) = q.
Moreover, for any linear isometry A : TpMŒ∫ ‚ÜíTqMŒ∫ we can modify f ‚ààGŒ∫ in
such a way that dpf = A (by composing with a suitable Àúf ‚ààGŒ∫ with Àúf( p) = p).
Since the isometry f is determined by f( p) and dpf, the group GŒ∫ contains all
isometries.

6.1
Let p be a point in the interior of the tetrahedron. Then each straight
half-line emanating from p will intersect the tetrahedron at exactly one point.

304
APPENDIX A
HINTS FOR SOLUTIONS
Thus the central projection with centre p will give a homeomorphism between
the tetrahedron and the sphere with centre p.
